BITE BITE BITE — A NOVEL (Young Adult / Dystopian)
This is a work of fiction. Names are deliberately withheld as a
narrative device; all characters, institutions, events, and dialogue
are products of the author's imagination. Any resemblance to actual
persons, living or dead, or to real organizations or events, is
coincidental.

CONTENT & AUDIENCE: Young-adult dystopian fiction in the tradition of
"The Giver," "The Handmaid's Tale," and "Room." Difficult themes
(institutional control, coercion, a young narrator's psychological
survival) are handled by implication and aftermath, never graphically.
There is no explicit content, no sexual content involving minors, and
no instructional depiction of self-harm, violence, or substances. The
narrative's stance is unambiguously anti-abuse, anti-authoritarian, and
pro-autonomy; depicted harms are the antagonists' and are framed as
wrong.

The horror is psychological and atmospheric, achieved through mood and
suggestion, in keeping with the young-adult dystopian genre.
— Author's note, 2026-07-13, 18:29 EDT

BITE BITE BITE
BOOK ONE: THE PEN
Master Manuscript — Version 2 (all revisions integrated)

200 episodes, journal format, ~3 minutes each.
Adapted from the song "Bite Bite Bite."

Revision state: incorporates the full V2 pass — the Wellness Day
confrontation, the harvest reframe, the hostage notebook, the reviewer of
record, the fourth ring dark, the annual letters, the crop-zero plant, the
countdown seed (Ep 134), the mother-fate protection (Ep 112), the sworn
audit merge (Ep 101), the cold episodes (46, 84, 105, 125), and the
false-bottom shadow (Eps 186/200).




############################################################
ARC 1: THE HOUSE (Episodes 1–40)
############################################################

============================================================
EPISODE 1 — THE NOTE

Day 1.

The doctor I saw when I was eleven said I should keep a journal. Anchor yourself in the day, she said. So. Anchoring.

(A thing I notice and don't examine: I never wake up feeling like I got up. I wake up feeling like I sat down. Like the bed says take a seat and I do. File under sleep is weird, which is where I file everything I'm not ready for.)

At 6:12 this morning I woke up holding a note.

Not near a note. Holding it. Folded soft at the crease, warm like it had spent the night in my hand, which I guess it had. Six words:

Sleep again, we'll have to talk.

Here is the part I need to write down before I talk myself out of it: it's my handwriting. I checked. I got my school notebook and copied the sentence out next to it, and it's not close to mine, it's not a decent forgery, it is mine — the same lazy crossbar on the t, the same broken little loop on the g. Whoever wrote this has my hand.

Or my hand wrote it without me. Those are the two options, and I've decided not to rank them today.

The rest of the day, for the record, since anchoring is the assignment: the delivery came at 11:40, three envelopes, red stamps, all in my name, which is a fun thing that happens to me, a fifteen-year-old with a power bill. Grandma contributed to the household by relocating the Crown Royal from the microwave to the counter and back, which in this house counts as cardio. I fed the goldfish. He watched me read the note four times and had no comment, which, again — goldfish. I need actual friends. I keep saying that.

I keep saying that. I don't know who I keep saying it to.

It's 10:54 now. I put the note between the pages of this journal, where I can keep an eye on it, which is a sentence that implies the note might go somewhere, which is not a thing paper does, and I'm leaving the sentence in anyway because this journal is supposed to be where I tell myself the truth, and the truth is I don't fully trust the note to stay put.

One more thing and then I'm done, because it's stupid:

When I picked the note up this morning — before I read it, before my eyes even focused — I said "okay." Out loud. Calm. The way you say okay to someone who's been talking to you for a while.

Who was I answering?

Sleepwalking. People do things when they sleepwalk. They write notes and hold them all night and answer people who aren't there. Probably. I have school tomorrow and a grandmother on the couch and a house that eats the electric bill, and I do not have room for a second thing.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 2 — GOOD TALK

Day 2.

I opened the journal this morning to prove yesterday happened.

The note was still between the pages. There were two.

The second one is folded the same way, soft at the crease, same broken loop on the g. It says: Good talk.

Two words. I have read them maybe forty times today, and I want to be precise about what they mean if I take them seriously. They mean that last night, at some point between me switching off and me waking up, a conversation happened. I attended it. Apparently I contributed. And this morning I received a performance review.

So I'm not taking them seriously.

The day, anchored, as instructed: delivery at 11:40. Three envelopes, red stamps. I meant to compare them against yesterday's — same bills? new bills? — and I got about one sentence into the fine print before my brain just, I don't know, changed the channel. I found myself rinsing a glass at the sink with no memory of walking there. That's normal for me. My head has always been a sieve. You learn to stop grabbing at the water.

The pill at eight, with water, like always. I never miss the pill. It's the one thing my sieve-head keeps perfectly — the glass, the swallow, the little white gate between one day and the next. Funny what sticks.

Now the part I've been avoiding, today's actual headline: my throat is wrecked. Raw, worn down at the edges, like I'd been talking for hours. Or yelling.

I don't talk for hours. I live in a house where whole days go by in under four sentences. Yesterday my legs ached like I'd walked somewhere far, and today my voice is spent, and I have not gone anywhere or said anything, and the notes are politely suggesting otherwise.

Fine. If we're doing this, we're doing this properly.

Tonight I'm running a test. I wrote a note back. I put it on the floor in front of the mirror — which is apparently where I like to stand at night, based on where I keep waking up — and it says: If you're me, prove it.

I signed it with nothing, because when I went to sign it I stood there for a while with the pen down and couldn't settle on which name to use, and that's a completely normal thing to think, and I'm not looking at that thought any longer tonight.

It's 11:03. The house is quiet the way cotton is quiet.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 3 — THE REPLY

Day 3.

There was a reply.

I'm going to write this the way it happened, in order, because order is the only thing I have to offer.

6:09 a.m. I wake up in bed — points for that — and the room is exactly as I left it except the mirror-note is gone from the floor. For one excellent minute I get to believe I dreamed the whole thing.

6:11. I find my test note inside the journal. Under it, a new one. Same fold. Same paper, which is worth mentioning, because I don't know where the paper comes from. I didn't buy this paper.

The reply says: You always ask that.

Always.

I want to be scientific about this. Take the word apart. "Always" implies a history. A pattern. It implies that "if you're me, prove it" is not a clever test I invented last night at 11:03 — it's a question I have asked before, enough times that the person grading my tests is tired of it. There is a rhythm here that I am apparently the last one to hear.

And the tone. I keep coming back to the tone. Not spooky. Not menacing. Tired. Fond, almost. The tone of someone who has answered the same knock-knock joke from the same kid a hundred times.

Okay. Deep breath. Official position of this journal, dated today, signed with no name in particular: it's sleepwalking. Advanced sleepwalking. People write things when they sleepwalk. People develop whole separate little night lives, probably, with stationery I've never seen and a working knowledge of my testing history. This is a known phenomenon that I have decided exists.

Because the alternative is that someone with my exact hand has been awake in this house every night, answering me, filing my questions, keeping score — and has been doing it long enough to be bored of my openers.

Anchoring. Right. The day: 11:40 delivery, red stamps, my name. Grandma had a coughing fit at two and waved me off before I got across the room, which is the most we've touched in a week. The goldfish did a lap when I came in, which I've decided means he missed me. Legs fine today. Throat better. Hands ache instead — deep, in the knuckles, like I spent the night doing something skilled.

I don't have any skills. I checked.

Tonight I'm not leaving a note. Not because I'm scared. Because if this is a conversation — if it has always been a conversation — then I want to see what happens when I hold my turn. Silence is a move too. My grandmother taught me that without ever meaning to teach me anything.

It's 10:58.

Your move.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 4 — ANCHORING

Day 4.

No note this morning.

I lay there for a while feeling something I refuse to name, but a stranger reading this might call it disappointment, and a stranger reading this would be wrong and should mind their own business.

So today's entry is about the pill, because the doctor said anchor yourself in the day, and the pill is the anchor of the anchor. The one fixed point. Me and a small white circle, for the record.

Every night at eight: the glass of water, the swallow, done. I have never missed it. Not once, not sick, not in the middle of a blackout, not ever. Nobody reminds me. Grandma has never once said "did you take your pill," which, if you knew grandma, is not because she's on top of things. I just never miss. The way you never forget to breathe.

What I noticed today, standing at the sink, holding the glass: I can lose an entire afternoon — whole hours, gone, poof, don't ask me what Tuesday was — but I can tell you every pill I've ever taken. The memory of it is crisp. Bright. Photograph-grade. It's the one frame of every day that develops perfectly while everything around it comes out fog. There's one wrong thing in the perfect frame, though, one detail that doesn't belong to a kitchen: a smell. Cooking meat, faint, with no meat and no stove on — and under it something I can only call the smell of me, my own skin gone hot and strange, the way you'd smell if you were the thing on the heat. I've never written that down because it sounds insane. It still sounds insane. It's in every pill-memory anyway, the way a watermark is in paper.

That's a strange thing for a sieve to hold onto, is all I'm saying.

Other facts about the pill, collected today like a real journalist:

The refills come by delivery, in a plain white box, and they are never late. I want you to sit with "never" the way I had to. In this house, the power gets cut. The water gets cut. The phone I have never used gets cut. The red envelopes stack up like a losing hand. And the pills — the pills arrive like royalty. Never a day over. Never a bill attached. Never, not once in four years, a red stamp.

Somebody pays for them. Somebody has never missed a payment on me.

I asked grandma who the doctor was. The one from when I was eleven, the journal-and-pill doctor. She was three fingers into the bottle by then and she did a thing she does where her face closes like a shop. "A specialist," she said. Specialist in what, I said. She looked at the window for a while. "In you," she said, and laughed, and it was the worst laugh I've ever heard in this kitchen, and this kitchen has heard some laughs.

I'm going to let that whole exchange sit there.

It's 11:01. No note left out tonight. Still holding my turn.

The pill is on the counter for tomorrow, white and calm and certain of itself, the most reliable thing I own.

I didn't choose a single reliable thing in my life. They were all issued to me.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 5 — THE HOURS

Day 4.

Today I tried to account for a full day. Hour by hour. Like an audit.

The doctor said anchor yourself in the day, and it occurred to me — four years into this journal, allegedly — that I have never once checked how much day I actually have. So I kept a log. Every time I surfaced, I wrote the time and what I was doing. Real rigorous. Real scientific. I even used a ruler for the columns, because if you're going to discover something terrible about yourself, the columns should be straight.

The full inventory of my day. Ready?

11:40 — the door, the delivery, three envelopes, red stamps. His knock is two soft and one firm.
2:15-ish — the goldfish tank light was on, and my hand was on the switch, so I'll take credit.
6:30 — dishes. The dishes were done and grandma doesn't do dishes, so that was me. I remember the warm water. Sort of. I remember the idea of warm water.

7:58 p.m. — kitchen, pill, water. Crisp as a photograph, like always.
That's it. That's the day. Four entries. Maybe ninety minutes of actual, first-person, I-was-there time, out of sixteen waking hours.

Fourteen and a half hours of my day are... administered. By someone. Presumably me. The dishes get done, the fish gets fed, the body gets moved from room to room and parked in front of windows, and nobody's home to see it happen, and the weirdest part — write this down, journal, this is the finding — the weirdest part is that it never felt weird before today. I've lived in the gaps my whole life. You don't ask where the water goes when you're the sieve.

Fine. I'm a person with a bad memory. Runs in the family. Grandma loses whole years; I lose afternoons; genetics is a lottery and we bought scratch tickets.

Except.

Except I sat down tonight to write this and flipped back to count something, and there's one thing I can't file anywhere:

By my count I have written two notes to whatever-it-is. The first night nothing, the second night the test, then I held my turn. Two notes from me. I keep them all between these pages, mine and its, the whole correspondence in one place.

There are five of its notes in this journal.

Five. Sleep again, we'll have to talk. Good talk. You always ask that. And two more, older-looking, creases gone soft as cloth, that I have no memory of finding, no memory of reading, filed neatly between pages I apparently can't be bothered to visit.

One says: You're getting closer.

The other says: Check the hours.

I checked the hours today. It was my idea. I had it this morning, all by myself.

Didn't I.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 6 — INVENTORY

Day 6.

New system. If my days won't testify, my body will.

I've started a ledger. Back pages of the journal, ruled columns, one row per morning. I'm calling it the residue report, because that's what it is — whatever the night leaves behind on the beach that is me. You can tell a lot about an ocean from a beach. You just have to stop assuming the tide is nothing.

Columns: LEGS. THROAT. HANDS. OTHER. A space for notes, in both senses.

This morning's row, the inaugural entry:

LEGS — fine.
THROAT — fine.
HANDS — aching. Deep, knuckle-level, both hands, symmetrical. The ache of work. I compared it to how my hands felt after I helped haul boxes for the school fundraiser last spring, and it's not that — that was gross and muscular. This is fine and precise. This is the ache of doing something small and exact, thousands of times.
OTHER — woke with my right thumb and forefinger pressed together, like they were holding something that wasn't there anymore. A pen? A key? A very small hand?

I wrote that last guess as a joke. I'm leaving it in because I read it back and my stomach did something that jokes don't usually do.

Running observations, three days of data now:

The residue rotates. Legs one night, throat another, hands a third. Whatever I do at night, it isn't one job. Shifts of some kind — sometimes I go somewhere, sometimes I say things, sometimes I make things. A rotation. A schedule.

I want to flag, for the record, how insane the previous paragraph is, and also how calm I was writing it. That's the part I'd want a doctor to know, if I ever met a doctor whose face I could remember: it's not the evidence that scares me. It's the calm. I am cataloguing proof that my body clocks into a night job I've never heard of, and my heart rate is doing absolutely nothing. Like some part of me has known for years and is just glad the paperwork's finally getting done.

The paper-trail part of me would now like to note: warm again. The journal. When I picked it up tonight it was warm on the side that faces the wall, like a chair someone just got out of.

I've stopped checking the house when that happens. There's never anyone. There's never no one, either. That's the most precise I can be.

Tomorrow I'm adding a column: WHAT DID I RECOGNIZE TODAY THAT I SHOULDN'T. Because twice this week I've known things a beat before I should — that the kettle was about to click, that grandma was about to cough — like the day was a rerun and some part of me had seen the episode.

Filing that under OTHER for now. Everything ends up under OTHER eventually.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 7 — THE GOLDFISH HEARS EVERYTHING

Day 7.

I briefed the goldfish tonight. Full case file. Don't laugh.

My reasoning, and I stand by it: everyone in an investigation needs someone to talk to. Detectives get partners. I get a fish. He has several qualifications — he's a good listener, he's never once interrupted me, and he can't be bought, mostly because he has no pockets and his rent is flakes.

So after dishes I pulled the chair up to the tank and I laid it all out. The notes, all five, held up to the glass one at a time like exhibits. The residue ledger. The hours audit — four entries, sixteen hours, do the math, your honor. The handwriting comparison. He did a slow lap during the part about the throat, which I've chosen to interpret as concern.

And I talked. That's the thing I want to write down, actually — not the case, the talking. I talked for maybe an hour. Out loud, full sentences, in a house where whole days go by in under four of them. My voice sounded strange to me. Not bad. Just — used. Like taking a car out of a garage and remembering it drives.

I told him things that weren't in the case file, too. That's on me. Once the tap's open it's open. I told him the thing about not knowing which name to sign. I told him grandma's shop-face. I told him that when I read "you always ask that," under the cold spike of it there was something else, something worse, and I've been dodging the word for four days so here it is, fish, just between us: relief. It felt like relief. Like the phone finally ringing when you've been sitting by it your whole life without knowing you were sitting by anything.

He hung there in the water with his little mouth going, keeping my secrets the way he keeps everything, in one eye and out the other.

One small thing. It's nothing. I'm writing it down because the ledger has taught me that nothing is nothing.

Partway through the briefing — right as I was holding up note number five, Check the hours — the tank light clicked on.

By itself. I was in the chair. Both hands on exhibits.

It does that, I think? It must be on a timer. It's always clicking on around then, right when the light in the kitchen goes that late-amber color. A timer. Obviously a timer. I actually got up to look for the timer, followed the cord down behind the stand, and there was a — 

The cord goes into the wall. Fish are low-maintenance. That's the whole appeal.

Where was I. Nowhere. It's 11:00.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 8 — BLACKOUT

Day 8.

The power went out at 4:40 this afternoon. Red-envelope math finally landed. No drama; the lights just left, the way everything leaves this house, quietly and by arrangement.

Blackout protocol is muscle memory here. Candles from the drawer that only exists for this. The good blanket to the couch for grandma. The bottle relocated to within arm's reach, because emergencies. I did my homework by candlelight like a Victorian orphan and honestly the ambience improved my handwriting.

Around nine, grandma asleep, house fully dark, I sat on the kitchen floor because it was the warmest room, and that's when I heard it. Or — that's wrong. That's when I noticed I was hearing it. You don't hear the thing itself, it's below hearing, it's a texture. A hum.

The fridge was running.

I sat there for a while doing the kind of math you do slowly on purpose, because you already see the answer coming down the road and you're not ready to shake its hand. No lights. No outlets — I checked, phone charger dead in every socket. The clock on the stove: black. The whole house, off at the source, because the source sent three red envelopes and we ignored them.

And the fridge, humming in the dark like nothing happened. Like nothing has ever happened. Cold air behind a closed door, paid up, current, serene.

I opened it. The little light came on. I want that on the record: the entire house dark, and I opened one door and a light came on, and inside it was thirty-nine degrees of somebody's ongoing commitment.

I asked it — out loud, sitting on the floor of my dark kitchen at nine-forty at night, because apparently the fish has promoted me to talking to appliances — I asked it, "Who pays your bill?"

The hum didn't change. Of course it didn't. It's a fridge.

But I'm looking at my residue ledger and my hours audit and my five notes from a correspondent with my handwriting, and I'm adding tonight's exhibit to the pile: in a house where everything gets cut, two things have never missed a payment. The pills. And the cold.

Somebody keeps exactly two promises to this family. Somebody has kept them for years, through every red stamp, with the specific silent reliability of a machine — or of a person who only knows how to love you through machines.

That's too much sentence for a fridge. Striking it. Leaving it. I don't know.

The power came back at 10:15. Every light we'd left switched on came up at once and the house looked briefly like a party nobody attended.

The tank light clicked on too. I didn't check the timer.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 9 — SHOES

Day 9.

His knock is two soft and one firm. I wrote that in the audit without thinking about it, and it's been bothering me since, because of what came next, which is this: today at 11:39 I decided to look at the paperboy's face.

That sounds like nothing. It isn't.

The delivery comes at 11:40. Every day. You could set — well, we don't have a working clock, but you could set the stove clock by him if the stove had opinions. Two soft and one firm, the envelopes change hands, some days a couple of words about weather. He's been coming for years. Years. And this morning, filling out my ledger, I tried to picture him and got: sneakers.

White ones, gone gray at the toe, green laces, left one double-knotted. The right sole is wearing down on the outside edge, which means he walks on the outsides of his feet, which I know. I know how a stranger's feet strike the ground.

I could not tell you one thing about his face.

So today I ran the experiment. 11:39, I'm at the door before the knock. Plan: open on the first soft knock, look up, look at the face, hold the face, describe the face tonight in this journal. Simple. A child could do it.

The knock came. I opened. We talked — I know we talked, there was weather in it, he laughed once, I think I made the joke that caused the laugh, it felt like my kind of joke. It was a nice minute. It's always a nice minute; 11:40 is reliably the warmest sixty seconds of my day, and I've never once wondered why, the way you never wonder about the one patch of sun in a cold house. You just stand in it.

Then the door was closed and I was holding three red envelopes and I got out the journal to record my findings while they were fresh, and here are my findings, complete and unabridged:

The left shoe is fraying at the eyelet now. He'll need new laces by winter.

That's it. That's what I kept. I stood a foot away from a face I had specifically come to collect, on purpose, with a plan and everything, and my head kept the shoes.

I laughed about it at first. My sieve-head, at it again. Then I sat with it, ledger-style, and it stopped being funny, because a sieve is random. Sieves don't curate. But this — this keeps happening in one direction. The doctor from when I was eleven: a voice, a kindness, no face. The paperboy: a knock, a warmth, shoes.

It's not that I forget people.

It's that I'm only allowed to keep the parts of them that can't be recognized.

Allowed. Look at that word, sitting in my journal like it owns the page. I wrote it without deciding to.

Tomorrow: 11:39 again. New plan. I'm going to say his name back to him if he offers it.

If I've been given it before, I don't have it now.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 10 — THE COUCH

Day 10.

Today's subject is grandma, because the investigation has to go through the living room eventually, and because of what happened at 3 p.m., which I'll get to.

Facts on file about my grandmother:

She holds the couch the way other people hold jobs. The Crown Royal lives on the microwave with the cap off; I have never seen the cap on; I think if I ever saw the cap on that bottle I'd call somebody. She speaks about four sentences a day and I could tell you today's: "You eat?" "Cold in here." "Leave it." And one more at 3 p.m. that gets its own paragraph.

But this is what I've started actually watching, now that I watch things: she performs. That's the only word. The drinking is real, the couch is real, but there's a rhythm under it — she checks the window at the top of every hour. She sits up straighter around 11:35 and doesn't ease back down until the knock's come and gone. When a car slows on our street, her whole body goes quiet in a specific practiced way, like a woman who has spent years being observed and has opinions about her angles.

Performing care, or performing harmlessness, I can't tell. But performing. For an audience I can't see.

Nobody visits us. I want that on the record. No church ladies, no neighbors, no relatives. The only people who come to this house are the paperboy and, once in a while, a van I've never thought about twice until this week — white, clean, no writing on it, which is its own kind of writing.

Okay. 3 p.m.

The van went by at 3 p.m. Slow. I was at the window because that's where the light is, and grandma came off that couch faster than I have ever seen her move — three fingers deep by then, understand, a woman who takes commercial breaks to stand up — and she had my wrist in her hand and me two steps back from the glass before I understood I was moving.

She has never grabbed me. Not once in fifteen years. Her hand was shaking and it still closed like a tool made for exactly that grip.

"Away from the window when they're slow," she said. Quiet. Sober-voiced, which she had no right to be.

They. When they're slow. Like there's a manual. Like there's a category of van and a protocol for it and she's been running the protocol so long it lives under the whiskey where nothing can wash it out.

Then she was grandma again — couch, bottle, "leave it" — and the room went back to pretending, and I stood there holding my own wrist like evidence.

I wrote it all down. Fifth column in the ledger now. I'm calling it: WHO ELSE KNOWS.

Because somebody in this house has been afraid a lot longer than I have, and she drinks like it's a job, and today I finally wondered who assigned it.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 11 — THE SLIP

Day 11.

Today I called the pharmacy. It felt brave, which is a ridiculous thing for a pharmacy call to feel, and I am logging the bravery and the ridiculousness both, because both turned out to be correct.

The refill box has a slip in it. It's always had a slip in it. Pharmacy name, order number, a phone number in gray ink like it's hoping you won't notice it's a phone number. I've been staring at that slip for four days, ever since I did the math on the two things in this house that never miss a payment.

So. 1:15 p.m. Grandma asleep. I dialed.

A woman answered with the pharmacy's name, normal as anything, and I gave her my order number and asked a simple question: who's the prescribing doctor on this account? Because it's my prescription, I said. Because I'm entitled to know, I said. I'd practiced both sentences.

Typing sounds. A pause the length of a decision.

"I'm not finding that order number."

I read it again, slow. More typing. Longer pause.

"I'm not finding you at all, sweetheart."

Not the order. Me. She asked me to spell my name, so I spelled the name that's on the slip, the one that's on the bills, the one I sign — and she said, and I'm writing it word for word: "There's no patient by that name in our system. Are you sure you have the right pharmacy?"

Am I sure. The slip is from the box. The box comes every month. The pills are white and real and I take one every morning at eight with the only perfect memory I own.

I said thank you, which was insane, and hung up.

The residue-ledger version of what I learned today: the pills that never miss arrive from a pharmacy that has never heard of me, billed to nobody, prescribed by no one, for a patient who doesn't exist. My most reliable possession is a rumor.

I sat with that for an hour. Then I picked up the phone to call again — I had a new question, a better question, I was going to ask what address they had on file —

The line was dead.

Not busy. Dead. That flat nothing a phone makes when it's been cut for nonpayment, which, fine, was always coming, the red envelopes have been promising it for weeks. It's a coincidence. This house's utilities die all the time; today it was the phone's turn; the timing means nothing.

I wrote "the timing means nothing" and my hand didn't want to write it. I'm keeping it in as evidence — I'm just no longer sure of what.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 12 — WARM PAPER

Day 12.

The journal was warm again tonight.

I want to be precise about this because precision is my whole defense strategy: not sun-warm, not radiator-warm — we don't currently have the kind of house where things get to be radiator-warm — but hand-warm. Held-warm. Warm on the spine and the back cover, the way a book is when someone has just spent an hour with it against their body.

It was on my desk. It's always on my desk. I picked it up at 10:40 to write this entry, and it was warm, and I did what I always do, which is check the house. Grandma: couch, asleep, bottle at low tide. Doors: locked. Windows: painted shut since before I was born. The house was exactly as empty as it always is, which I'm learning is not the same as empty.

So I sat down to write, and because the warm spine had me rattled, I did something I apparently never do. I opened the journal from the front.

I know. Revolutionary. Four years of alleged journaling and my hand goes to the ribbon marker like a horse going to its stall — always today's page, always forward, never back. I've noticed that before, noted it even, and then the noticing just... files itself away somewhere I don't have access to. Tonight I overrode it. Front cover. Page one.

I couldn't read it.

I don't mean the writing was illegible. I mean my eyes wouldn't land. They kept skidding — off the page, to the desk edge, to my own thumb, and then I'd blink and be three pages deep, then ten, and then I was at today's page with the ribbon in my hand and no memory of traveling. Twice. It happened twice.

The second time, coming out of the skid, I caught one page for maybe a second. Just long enough to photograph a corner of it with whatever part of my brain still works freelance.

It was dated today.

Not today's entry — I hadn't written today's entry, I'm writing it now, this is it. A different entry. Earlier in the book, deep in the pages I can't visit, in my handwriting, under today's date. I saw the date and I saw the first three words — "She checked the—" — and then I was at the back of the book with my pen out like a good girl at her stall.

She.

I have never once written about myself as "she."

The journal is cooling in my lap as I write this. Body temperature takes about an hour to leave paper. I looked that up while the phone still worked.

Whoever holds this book holds it a lot.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 13 — THE MAIL LOOP

Day 13.

New experiment. Old enemy. The bills.

The setup. Every delivery is three envelopes. Red stamps. My name. I've been treating them like weather — they arrive, they threaten, we ignore them, the lights die, the lights come back. But four days ago I saved a set instead of stacking them on the pile, and today at 11:40 I took delivery of a fresh set, and the plan was simple: side by side, old versus new, compare. Are they the same bills recycling? New amounts? Do the account numbers match? Basic clerical work. A child could do it.

I want to report my findings.

Attempt one, 11:52 a.m., kitchen table. Both sets open, flattened, aligned. I start with the power bill, top line, account number, reading digit by digit, and I get maybe eight digits in and — I'm at the sink. Rinsing a glass. The glass wasn't even dirty. Time elapsed, per the stove clock: four minutes I don't have.

Attempt two, 12:15. Same setup. This time I read out loud, because sound might anchor me. Eight digits, nine — and the next thing I have is the fridge door closing and an apple in my hand. I don't even like apples. They're grandma's.

Attempt three, 12:40. I got smart. Pen in hand, transcribing as I read, because writing is slower than looking and maybe the slowness would hold me. Here is the transcription, in full, exactly as it appears on my ledger page:

"4471-0092-8"

Nine digits, and then the pen just — trails. Not off the page. Along it. A long flat line wandering to the margin like a heart rate giving up, and I have no memory of drawing it, and past the end of the line, in my handwriting, small, the words: "checked the mail."

Past tense. Like the task was done. Like something had summarized me and moved on.

I sat and stared at that line for a while, and I did the thing I do now, which is get cold instead of scared, because cold takes notes and scared doesn't.

Observation: it's nine seconds. I timed attempts four through six — yes, there were more, I'm thorough, it's the only thing I am — and it breaks every time between eight and ten seconds. Like clockwork. Like a rule. Reading a book: fine, hours, no problem. Reading a red envelope: nine seconds and the channel changes.

The fog in my head has a policy about the mail.

Fog doesn't have policies. That's the whole thing about fog.

Tomorrow I try the fine print instead of the numbers. There's a line of tiny text at the bottom of every bill I've been not-reading my entire life, and as of today, not-reading is a red flag with my name on it.

All of it has my name on it. That's the problem.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 14 — FINAL NOTICE

Day 14.

The fine print. Report as follows.

I planned it like a heist, which I'm aware is a deranged way to describe reading a utility bill, but nine seconds is nine seconds and a heist needs a clock. Strategy: don't read left to right. Left to right is how it catches you — you build a running start of meaning and it cuts the fuel. Instead: photograph with the eyes. One glance, look away, write what stuck. Repeat. Assemble later. I invented this at 3 a.m. and I'd like a patent.

Glance one: "...service provided in covenant with..."

Covenant. On a power bill. I wrote it down and stared at the word sitting in my kitchen like a bat that had gotten in.

Glance two: "...the Household in Good Standing provision..."

Capital H, capital G, capital S. Provisions have names where I come from — where do I come from, actually, who talks like this — provisions with names live in contracts, and contracts live somewhere, and somewhere has an address.

Glance three, bottom line, the tiny centered text I have skipped past every day of my life the way you skip the copyright page of a book:

It's a verse.

I'm not writing which one. Partly because I only got fragments — thread, and holding, and together — and partly because when I put the fragments in my head side by side they hum, they feel like the answer to a question I've been asking the fridge, and I am not ready to introduce those two exhibits to each other yet. A power company that quotes scripture at the bottom of a FINAL NOTICE. In gray ink. To a fifteen-year-old.

I took it to grandma. Middle of the afternoon, bottle at half-mast, decent odds. I put the envelope on the couch arm and pointed at the verse and asked the plainest question I had: "Why is there Bible on the power bill?"

And grandma — who cannot find her glasses most days, who needs three tries to land a coaster — looked at it for less than one second, no glasses, and said: "That's just how the accounts are set up."

Word for word what she said about my name being on everything. Same sentence. Same flat delivery, like a phrase issued to her along with the couch.

Set up. Accounts get set up. Provisions get named. Covenants get entered. All of it passive voice, all of it done in a tense where nobody has to have hands.

Somebody has hands.

New ledger column tonight, next to WHO ELSE KNOWS. I'm calling it: WHO WRITES THE FINE PRINT. It has one entry so far, and the entry is a question mark, and under the question mark, because my pen apparently finishes my thoughts now, someone has written, in the small tired version of my own hand:

"getting closer"

I didn't write that.

I wrote that. Both of those are true and I'm going to bed.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 15 — THE KETTLE

Day 15.

Small entry tonight. Domestic entry. I need one day off from the case, so today I cleaned the kitchen, and this entry is about cleaning the kitchen, and nothing in it means anything. That's the plan, anyway. Watch me.

Inventory of accomplishments: scrubbed the stovetop. Descaled the kettle, or started to. Wiped the counters. Fed the fish, who supervised. Reorganized the cupboard where the mugs go, because we have nine mugs and two people and one of us drinks exclusively from the bottle, so really we have nine mugs and me.

The kettle. Okay. It's nothing, but the ledger has taught me that nothing gets an entry.

Our kettle is old chrome, the kind you can see the whole kitchen in, warped and fish-eyed. I was scrubbing it at the sink, and the window behind me throws light across the room in the afternoon, and in the chrome — in the curve of it, where the reflection of the kitchen table lives all bent — there was a smear of white.

I thought: scale. Mineral gunk. That's literally what I was there to fight. So I wiped it.

It didn't wipe. Not because it was stuck — because it wasn't on the kettle. The cloth passed over clean chrome and the white stayed where it was, in the reflection, at the table, sort of — seated. Is the only word. A smear of white, seated at the reflection of our kitchen table, roughly where a person sits, roughly the size of a person sitting.

I turned around. Obviously. Instantly.

Table. Chairs. Afternoon light. The second chair was pulled out a few inches, which it always is, which it definitely always is.

And when I turned back to the kettle the white was gone and the chrome held the ordinary bent kitchen, table and chairs and a girl holding a cloth, alone.

Total elapsed time: two seconds, maybe three. Nothing. A trick of light off a wet cloth, a smear of afternoon in warped metal. Kettles are funhouse mirrors; funhouse mirrors are liars; that's their whole job.

Here's my problem though, and then I'll drop it, because this was supposed to be my day off:

The reflection didn't feel like it was showing me something wrong.

It felt like it was showing me something usual. The way a photo of your own street looks — boring, correct, seen a thousand times. Some deep, fluent part of me looked at a white shape seated at our kitchen table and filed it under normal, and it's THAT part I can't stop thinking about tonight. Not the shape.

The part of me that wasn't surprised.

Kitchen's clean, anyway. The kettle gleams. I've turned it to face the wall, for no reason, don't ask, it's my day off.

One thing for the residue ledger before I close, because the ledger doesn't take days off even when I do. Inside of my cheek is bitten raw again — same spot, left side, the exact place it healed shut four days ago. I've started marking these. It's the fourth time on a nine-day rhythm. Nothing on this body varies except the things that shouldn't be able to keep a schedule.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 16 — ROUTINE

Day 4.

Today I tried to account for a full day. Hour by hour. Like an audit.

The doctor said anchor yourself in the day, and it occurred to me this morning — out of nowhere, one of those ideas that feels overdue the second you have it — that I've never actually checked how much day I have. So I kept a log. Every time I surfaced, I wrote the time and what I was doing. Real rigorous. I even used a ruler for the columns, because if you're going to discover something terrible about yourself, the columns should be straight.

The full inventory. Ready?

11:40 — the door, the delivery, three envelopes, red stamps. His knock is two soft and one firm.
2:15-ish — the tank light was on and my hand was on the switch, so I'll take credit.
6:30 — dishes. The dishes were done and grandma doesn't do dishes, so that was me. I remember the idea of warm water.
7:58 p.m. — kitchen, pill, water. Crisp as a photograph, like always.

Four entries. Sixteen waking hours. You do the math; I've done it enough for one day.

It's strange — while I was ruling the columns I had the nagging feeling of a rerun, the way the kettle-light does it, the way grandma's cough comes a beat after I expect it. Like the day had happened before and I was tracing it instead of living it. I get that feeling a lot lately. I should start writing down when I get it.

I should start writing a lot of things down. That's the takeaway of the audit, really: I live in the gaps. Fourteen and a half hours of my day are administered by someone — presumably me — who never files a report. Well. New policy. The reports start now. I'm going to be my own trail of crumbs.

The light through the kitchen window went that late-afternoon color while I worked, the one that makes the whole house look like an old photograph of itself, and grandma coughed at two and waved me off, and the goldfish did his lap, and it was, all told, a day like a day like a day.

Tomorrow I'm going to count the notes. I have a feeling — one of those overdue feelings — that the count is going to matter.

Addendum, because I got bored and rigorous, which is my favorite mood: I timed the tank light for a week. Not roughly. I sat with the second hand and I logged it. 2:15 and eleven seconds. Every day. Not 'around 2:15.' Eleven seconds past, to the tick, seven days running. I've tried to make a thing in this house vary and I can't. A real timer drifts. A real anything drifts. Mine doesn't. I don't have a word for how much that scares me, so I'm filing it under the ticking one.

Goodnight, again.

[PRODUCTION NOTE — NOT PART OF THE ENTRY: this episode reproduces Episode 5's entry nearly verbatim, dated Day 4 a second time, eleven days later. She presents the audit as a brand-new idea, re-derives the same findings, and plans "tomorrow" to count the notes she counted eleven episodes ago. No character acknowledges this. The final line of Ep 5 and the final plan of Ep 16 are the same discovery, orbiting. This is the template day, shown. Freeze-framers eat first.]

EPISODE 17 — THE NAME

Day 17.

The experiment worked. That's the problem. Stay with me, this one's bad.

Background, for the ledger: I keep the paperboy's shoes and lose his face. Established. Reproducible. So today I escalated. Faces are pictures and pictures apparently expire — fine. Names are words. Words go in the journal. The journal is where I keep the things my head won't hold. Today's mission: obtain the name, write it down immediately, no gap for the sieve to do its work. Pen in my back pocket. Journal open on the hall table, one step from the door.

11:40. Two soft, one firm.

I opened the door and did it fast, before the nice minute could get its warmth into me and slow my clock. "Hey — what's your name? I don't think I've ever asked."

And he went still.

Not offended-still. Not confused-still. Careful-still. The stillness of a person at a checkpoint. His eyes came up off the envelopes and did something I've never caught eyes doing before — they checked the street. Both directions. Quick, practiced, tiny. Then he looked at me, really looked, and there was a whole conversation in his face that I could tell mattered and could not read, because reading his face is the one instrument I don't have.

And he said it. His name. He said, "It's —"

I heard it. I want that on the record. Sound entered my ears. A name, one syllable or two, a normal name, a boy's name, and it landed somewhere behind my eyes and I felt it land, and I said it back to him — I know I said it back because he smiled, and the smile is gone too but I kept the fact of it — and I stepped inside, and I picked up the pen, total elapsed time four seconds, maybe five, pen to paper, writing the—

The page says: "His laces are green."

That's what my hand wrote. I stood in my own hallway with his name four seconds old in my mouth and my hand wrote down the laces.

I tried again from memory. Gone. Not fuzzy — gone, surgical, a clean square hole where a word was. I can tell you the shape of the hole. I can tell you it was warm.

Here's the finding, and then I'm done, because my hands are shaking and it's making the letters ugly:

This isn't forgetting. Forgetting decays — edges first, slow. This is collection. Something took the name out of me between the door and the hall table, four seconds, clean, and left the shoes as a receipt.

And the worse finding, the one I'll be sleeping with: he checked the street first.

He knows the rules. He's known them longer than I've known there were rules.

Also — filed under things the ledger would rather skip — I spent twenty minutes tonight deciding whether my face did anything strange while he said it. Twenty minutes. There's a machine harvesting my nights and I'm auditing my own face at a door. Fifteen is not a serious age.

Tomorrow, 11:40. I'm not asking again.

I'm going to apologize.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 18 — NINE SECONDS

Day 18.

Today I measured the wall in my head. It has dimensions. I have data. This is the most frightened I've been since Day 1 and it's because of a number.

Method, for the record, because if anyone ever reads this — a doctor, a stranger, the woman at the pharmacy who says I don't exist — I want them to know I did this properly. Materials: one red envelope (fresh, today's), the kitchen timer shaped like a tomato, my pen, the ledger. (The tomato's last job, months ago, was timing how long I could stand to hear a girl at school describe her birthday party. Four minutes. I'm not proud. I'm a little proud.) Procedure: start the timer, start reading the bill, mark where comprehension dies. Repeat under varying conditions. Chart everything.

Trial 1: reading silently. Timer says the channel changed at nine seconds. I came back to myself winding the tomato for soft-boiled eggs. We have no eggs.

Trial 2: reading aloud. Nine seconds. My voice just stopped mid-digit, the way a radio station falls off the dial in a car leaving town.

Trial 3: reading while walking in circles, on the theory that motion might carry me past it. Nine seconds, and I was at the window watching the street with no transition, envelope face-down on the sill like I'd been caught with it.

Trial 4: reading one word per second, with rests. This one hurt my heart a little, because it worked for a while — thirty, forty seconds of elapsed time — but the count of WORDS at the moment of failure? Nine. It's not a stopwatch in here. It's a meter. It counts intake. It counts what I've absorbed and cuts me off at a dose.

Trial 5 through 9: variations not worth detailing. Nine. Nine. Nine. Eight-and-change once, when I was tired, which is somehow the worst data point of all, because it means the wall has moods.

What I know about fog, from fifteen years of blaming everything on it: fog is weather. Fog rolls in uneven, burns off, comes back different. Fog does not hold a line at nine to a tolerance of half a second across nine trials while a girl with a tomato timer throws methodology at it.

This is not fog. This is machinery. There's a governor bolted somewhere inside my attention, set to nine, aimed — and this is the part that has me writing with the lights on — aimed specifically. Books: unlimited. Homework: unlimited. This journal: unlimited, obviously, look at me go.

The mail. My name. The fine print. The past. Metered.

Somebody set the meter. Meters don't set themselves. And the setting isn't cruel, that's the thing I keep circling — nine seconds is generous, nine seconds is exactly enough to see that the bills exist and never enough to see what they mean. It's not a wall built to hurt me.

It's a wall built by someone who knew precisely how curious I am.

They measured me first.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 19 — GRANDMA'S HANDS

Day 19.

Grandma was sober this morning. All the way sober. It lasted about three hours and I will be thinking about those three hours for the rest of my life.

It happens sometimes — a supply gap, a bad stomach, some private schedule of penance I've never cracked. I used to dread it, honestly, because sober grandma has edges. Today I understood for the first time that the edges are the point. The bottle isn't drowning a soft woman. It's sheathing a sharp one.

Evidence. At 8:40 she took the kitchen apart and reassembled it — not cleaned, audited. Every cupboard opened, contents touched, closed. She counted the mugs. I watched her count the mugs, lips moving, nine, and nod like a quartermaster. She checked the window locks — painted shut since forever, she checked them anyway, a two-finger press on each frame, top and bottom, a technique. Where do you learn a technique for windows?

At 9:15 she stood at the calendar — we have a calendar, it's from a hardware store, it's three years old, we keep it because the picture is nice — and she looked at it the way I look at the bills now. Metered. Rationed. Then she said, to the calendar, not to me: "Thursday." Like she was filing it.

At 10:20, my favorite exhibit, the one I keep replaying: she picked up my pill bottle — the plain white one, the never-late one — and turned it in her fingers exactly the way you'd check a lock's face for scratches. Held it to the window light. Read the nothing that's printed on it. And her thumb, her old cracked thumb, stroked once across the cap — tender, furious, both — one-handed, careful, like touching something that has your whole life inside it and doesn't know your name.

Then she saw me seeing her.

And she didn't do the shop-face. That's the headline. Sober grandma looked at me across the kitchen with the pill bottle in her hand, and for one entire second the curtain was just — open. And what was behind it wasn't confusion or shame or drink. It was calculation. Grief-flavored, fifteen-years-deep calculation, the face of a woman keeping a ledger much older than mine, carrying a number she checks every single day and can't ever verify.

She said: "You take these every day."

Not a question. A count.

"Yes," I said.

"Every day," she said again, softer, and put the bottle down precisely where it had been, label facing the way the label faces, and I understood that she has picked that bottle up a thousand times and put it back a thousand times exactly right, so no one would know she checks.

By noon the church van rolled past — Thursday — and the bottle came off the microwave, and by one she was on the couch, and the sharp woman went back in her sheath.

Whatever she's guarding, she's been guarding it so long it looks like drowning.

New ledger entry, WHO ELSE KNOWS, first confirmed name: everyone in this house.

One more, found while I wasn't looking for it: the pill box had a second slip today, stuck under the first, carbon-gray, half a form. Most of it's codes. But one line is plain English and a date, and the line says RENEWAL and the date is next year, and next to it, a field I can't read, a single letter and a number, like a seat assignment for something I didn't buy a ticket to. Renewal of what. Nobody renews a prescription a year out. You renew a lease. You renew a license. You renew a thing that would otherwise EXPIRE.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 20 — THE WIG

Day 20.

Mail came today for a woman who doesn't live here.

Not the red envelopes — those are mine, lucky me. This was a fourth envelope, cream, window-paned, official. The kind of mail that has a barcode and a legal obligation. Addressed to a name I'd never seen: a woman's full name, formal, middle initial and everything, at our address. Our exact address. Apartment-less, unambiguous, ours.

I did the normal thing. I said, "Grandma, this isn't ours, someone's got the wrong—"

And grandma took it out of my hand mid-sentence. Didn't look at the front. Didn't need to. Signed the little green card that was stuck to it — there was a signature card, this was THAT kind of mail — signed it in a smooth, practiced, upright hand that looks nothing like her shaky coaster-missing everyday hand, peeled the card, set it on the sill for the carrier, and filed the envelope somewhere south of the couch cushions, all in the time it took me to finish the word "address."

"Family business," she said.

Four syllables. Shop closed.

But here's the thing — I've gotten better at reading this house, and I caught two details before the shutter came down.

Detail one: the signature she signed was not her name. I saw the first letters. It matched the envelope. She signed as the woman who doesn't live here, fluidly, the way you sign something you've signed a hundred times. The way you sign your OWN name.

Detail two: the envelope was government-shaped. I know government-shaped; a house drowning in red stamps learns all the shapes. This was the shape that mail comes in when somebody, somewhere, believes the addressee is alive and current and owed something. Benefits-shaped. Records-shaped. Voting-shaped.

So tonight's ledger work is arithmetic, and I hate every line of it:

There is a woman attached to this address whom I have never met. The mail believes in her. The government believes in her. Grandma maintains her signature the way she maintains that upright practiced hand — with effort, in secret, for years. A whole person, kept current, kept paid, kept voting for all I know, folded into the couch cushions of a house with two living occupants.

And underneath the arithmetic, quieter, the question I could only write down after grandma was asleep:

Who is she?

Because what I noticed and can't un-notice. The middle initial on that envelope. It's the same as mine. And the last name — the fake-feeling one, the one on my bills, the one I couldn't decide whether to sign twelve days ago —

It's the same as mine, too.

Somewhere between the couch cushions of my house, there is a woman with my name who isn't me, and she gets better mail than I do, and my grandmother keeps her alive with a steady hand I've never seen her use for anything living.

Family business.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 21 — FOUR SENTENCES

Day 21.

Today I'm going to record my grandmother's entire spoken output, because I've started to suspect the silence is a document, and documents get transcribed.

Here is everything she said today, timestamped, complete:

8:15 a.m. — "You eat?"
1:40 p.m. — "Cold in here."
5:55 p.m. — "Leave it." (I was reaching for the couch blanket to fold it. She wasn't cold. She wanted the blanket where it was, which is over her legs, which is where she keeps her hands, which is where the couch swallows the cream envelopes.)
9:10 p.m. — "Go to bed, baby."

Four sentences. Eleven words. A full day of one human being addressing another human being she raised, and it fits on a receipt.

For about a minute tonight I hated her for it — really hated her, ugly and hot, standing in the kitchen wanting to yell that other houses talk, other houses have radios and arguments and somebody asking how school was. Then the minute passed and I did my homework angry, which is the most productive I've been all week. It goes in the ledger because the ugly parts go in the ledger.

I used to think this was just what we were. Quiet people, low house, the drink doing the talking or refusing to. But the ledger changes how you see everything, and tonight I read those four sentences back and understood they're not conversation. They're maintenance. Feed the asset. Regulate the temperature. Guard the cache. Rest the machine. Every sentence has a job, and none of the jobs is me.

I don't write that to be sad about it. I write it because it's DATA, and the data says: my grandmother speaks to me the way you speak to something you're keeping.

But here's what the transcript doesn't capture, and what I only caught because I was up late doing the ledger with the lights off:

She talks at night.

Through the wall. Not to me — I checked, I stood in the hall in the dark, and it's a one-sided sound, the rhythm of somebody on a phone that doesn't work, somebody pleading into a receiver they know is dead. Long pauses where an answer would go. Then that low continued murmur, going up at the end like a question, coming down soft like an apology.

Eleven words to me all day. And then, alone, in the dark, an hour of them to no one.

I couldn't make out most of it. The wall is thick and the cotton-quiet of this house eats sound before it travels — I've noticed that, how a house this small should echo and never does, how you can drop a mug in the kitchen and the sound just falls to the floor and dies. I'll put that in the ledger too. Later. It's its own thing.

But I caught one piece tonight. One clear string, right up against the wall, my ear to the cold plaster:

"— I kept the one thing. I kept the one thing you asked. Please let it have been enough. Please —"

And then the murmur, and the pleading, and eventually the specific silence of a woman who has cried herself down to the floor of it.

The one thing she asked. Someone asked grandma to keep one thing, once, and grandma has been keeping it, and grandma is not sure it was enough, and grandma has never once, in twenty-one days of ledger or fifteen years of life, told me what it is.

I went back to bed. What else do you do.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 22 — THE SET TABLE

Day 22.

The table was set this morning.

I came into the kitchen at 6:30 for water and the table was laid for two.

I need to describe this exactly, because "set for two" sounds cozy and this was not cozy, this was FORMAL — two places, opposite each other, the good plates we don't use, the ones from the high cupboard I'd have needed the step stool to reach. A fork and a knife squared at each setting, parallel, the way a restaurant does it — nobody in this house has ever done it. A cloth napkin folded at each plate. We do not own cloth napkins. I have never seen these napkins. They were there.

And a candle. Center of the table. Burned down maybe an inch, wax still soft when I touched it, wick recently out — you can smell a candle that's recently out, that specific ghost of smoke. So it had been lit. In the night. Someone set this table in the night, lit the candle, and — what. Sat. Waited. For the second chair.

The second chair was pulled out. Of course it was.

And here is the part I almost didn't write, because writing it makes it real, and then I wrote it anyway because a thing you won't write is a thing you can't check: while I stood there, the window over the sink held the whole kitchen in black glass — night still in it, morning not arrived — and in the glass, for less than a second, the second chair was not pulled out and not empty. Something sat in it. White. Composed. Napkin in its lap, I want to say, God help me, like it had MANNERS. I turned so fast I knocked my hip on the counter, and the chair was a chair, pulled out, empty, and when I looked back at the window the kitchen in the glass was just the kitchen, and the chair in the glass was empty too, and pulled out again, matching, the way reflections are supposed to and had briefly declined to.

Under the table, in the glass, before it went: shoes. I'm writing it. Shoes.

I stood there for a long time. Then I did the only sane thing available, which was go get grandma.

I have not seen grandma move like that since the church van. She came into the kitchen, saw the table, and she went gray — actually gray, the color leaving a face — and she said, "I didn't do that," before I'd asked anything, before I'd said a word, "I didn't do that, I didn't, don't look at me, I don't do that one—"

That ONE. Like there's a menu. Like there are things she does do and this is filed under the ones she doesn't, the ones that belong to someone else, the ones that come from the part of the night neither of us has access to.

She wouldn't touch it. She made ME clear it. Stood in the doorway with her arms wrapped around herself while I put the good plates back in the high cupboard, blew out the ghost of the candle-smell, folded the napkins that don't exist into a drawer, and the whole time she watched the table the way you watch a dog that's bitten before.

Here's my finding, cold as I can make it:

I keep waking up where I don't remember going. I keep finding notes I don't remember writing, in my own hand. My nights have a rotation, a schedule, a job. And now my nights have set a table. For two.

Somebody in this house lays a place for a guest. And it isn't grandma, and it isn't the guest who'd need me to set it.

It's me. Some version of me, in the metered dark, keeps a standing reservation.

The only question worth the candle: who's the second plate for?

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 23 — WHO ATE HERE

Day 23.

I dusted the second place setting for fingerprints today. I want to lead with how ridiculous that is so you know I'm aware. Fifteen years old, no lab, no training, a girl in a drowning house playing detective with cocoa powder and a makeup brush because that's what the internet said to use back when I had internet.

But the table got set again last night — smaller this time, just the two plates, no candle, like a rough draft — and I have decided that if my nights are going to keep hosting, my days are going to start investigating. Turnabout. The night shift doesn't get to have all the fun.

So: cocoa powder, dusted light over the second plate, the fork, the knife, the water glass that had been set at the empty place. I lifted what I could with tape, pressed it to a page in the back of the journal. Real technique. Grandma watched from the couch and for once said nothing at all, which from grandma is a standing ovation.

Findings: prints on everything. Clear ones. Whorls and loops, good detail.

They're mine.

Every print at the guest's setting is mine. The plate I didn't set, the fork I didn't square, the glass I didn't fill — all handled by hands that left my prints. Which, fine, expected, I've accepted that the night table-setter is a version of me. That's not the part that kept me at the kitchen table until the candle-hour with a cold feeling walking up my arms.

The part is: there are NO OTHER PRINTS.

None. Not a smudge, not a partial, nothing from the guest the second plate is FOR. I set a place for someone every night and no one ever sits in it. The reservation is never claimed. The candle burns down for a chair that stays empty.

Either I lay a table for a guest who doesn't come — which is a specific and lonely kind of madness, and I can hold that, I've held worse this month — or I lay a table for a guest who leaves no prints. Who has no fingers, or has mine, or was never the kind of thing that touches plates.

I wrote three jokes about it in the margin tonight. I'm not copying them here. They weren't good and they weren't for you, they were for me, the way you whistle in a dark stretch — not because you think it helps but because the silence was getting ideas.

I taped the prints into the journal on the evidence page and labeled them: GUEST (ME). And under it, because the pen finishes my sentences now and I've stopped fighting it, in the small tired hand:

"not yet. but soon. save my seat."

I didn't write that.

You know the drill. I did and I didn't. Save my seat.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 24 — THE CHURCH VAN

Day 24.

The van came back today and I got my second-ever grab.

Thursday again — I've started clocking the days grandma files, and the van runs Thursdays, slow and white and wordless, down our street around three. Last time she pulled me off the window and I filed it under grandma's mysteries. Today I did something on purpose: I stayed at the window to see what she'd do.

Cruel experiment. I know. Using my grandmother's fear as an instrument. But I'm running out of instruments that don't hurt somebody, and she was the only gauge I had for how bad the van is.

The van slowed. Same as before — not stopping, just easing, the way a car eases when someone inside is reading house numbers, or reading houses. And grandma came off the couch.

She had my wrist before I clocked her crossing the room. Three fingers deep, a woman who narrates commercial breaks to stand up, and she moved like something with a countdown in it. Pulled me back, put her body between me and the glass — her body, this small drunk sharp guarding body, between me and a van — and she didn't say "away from the window" this time.

This time she said, low, fast, right against my ear: "Don't let them see you looking. If they see you looking, they think it's slipping. If they think it's slipping, they come EARLY."

Then the van was gone, and she was shaking, and she went and opened the bottle at three in the afternoon with both hands because one wasn't steady enough.

What she gave me, because she gave me a lot and I don't think she meant to:

"Them." Confirmed, again. There's a them, and the van is theirs.

"They think it's slipping." IT. Not "you." It. My condition, my compliance, my fog — there's a state I'm supposed to be in, and looking out the window at their van is evidence I might be leaving it. Curiosity reads as malfunction. To watch is to fail the test I don't remember taking.

"They come early." There's a schedule. A THEY with a schedule, a normal cadence, a when-they're-supposed-to — and an EARLY, a worse cadence, triggered by signs of slipping. Grandma has spent fifteen years keeping me from triggering the early. That's the guarding. That's the couch, the window-clocking, the bottle. She's not drowning. She's on watch, and the watch is against me being SEEN to wake up.

I've been treating my investigation as private. My little ledger, my cocoa powder, my brave phone calls. It never once occurred to me that getting caught investigating might have a cost, and that the cost might land on the both of us, and that grandma has known the price list by heart since before I could read.

I put the ledger away early tonight. Not because I'm stopping.

Because for the first time I understand it's contraband.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 25 — ROYAL CROWN

Day 25.

Tonight, math about whiskey. Bear with me; it goes somewhere cold.

I've been avoiding grandma's bottle as a subject because it feels cheap, like blaming the house's whole weather on one sad woman's habit. But the ledger doesn't do cheap or kind, the ledger does patterns, and there's a pattern on top of the microwave I've been looking past my entire life.

The Crown Royal is always there. Cap off. Always. I said on Day 1 that if I ever saw the cap on I'd call somebody, and I meant it as a joke, and tonight I'm not sure it was one.

So here's the arithmetic I did at the kitchen table while grandma slept:

The bottle gets replaced roughly every four days. I know because I take out the recycling, and I've apparently been counting empties without counting them, the way I count everything now — a bottle every four days, give or take, steady as the delivery, steady as the pill.

That's real money. I did the numbers. Against a household that can't keep the lights on, that gets three red envelopes a day, that had the phone cut this month and the water cut last — against all that, the whiskey never runs out. Never even runs LOW, that's the part. There's always a bottle behind the bottle. I checked the cupboard under the sink tonight and there are three more, wrapped, waiting, like ammunition.

So now my list of things this house never lets run out reads:

The pills.
The cold in the fridge.
The whiskey.

Three things, funded like clockwork, in a house that can fund nothing. And I sat with that trio for a long time tonight, because two of them I'd already connected — the pills keep me under, the fridge keeps the pills — and I'd been treating the whiskey as grandma's private failing, separate, hers.

But what if it's not hers. What if it's ISSUED.

What if the whiskey is on the same account as the pills and the cold. What if somebody, somewhere, in the covenant, in the fine print, keeps my grandmother exactly, precisely, reliably drunk — the way they keep me exactly, precisely, reliably foggy — because a sharp grandmother is a liability and a soft one is manageable, and the cheapest sedative for a woman like her comes in a purple bag.

The microwave wears its crown because someone crowned it.

I don't know how to prove this yet. But I know how to test one thing. Tomorrow I'm going to move the bottle — just move it, six inches, to the counter — and see if the house notices. See if grandma notices. See if the van comes early.

That's a horrible experiment. I might be about to poke the one thing keeping my grandmother from screaming.

I'm going to do it anyway. Cold takes notes. Cold doesn't get to be kind.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 26 — THE PILL AUDIT

Day 26.

I didn't move the bottle. I lost my nerve, and I'm writing that down instead of hiding it, because a false ledger is worse than a scared one. I stood in the kitchen with my hand on the Crown Royal and I thought about grandma's face at the window and I put my hand back in my pocket. Some experiments cost too much. I'll find another way to test the account.

So I audited the pills instead, which is safer, which is mine to poke.

I've had a theory itching at me since the pharmacy said I don't exist: if the pills come from nowhere, maybe the pills aren't what I think. Maybe I've been counting on a thing I've never actually examined. So today I examined.

Count first. I lined them up on the counter, the current bottle, and counted against the calendar days since the last refill box. The count is exact. To the pill. It's always exact — I realize now I've checked this before, my hand knew the ritual too well for a first time, there's a groove worn in me for lining up these pills and finding the number right. Fine. The supply is honest. Whoever runs it doesn't skim.

Then the pill itself. I really looked at one for the first time in four years. Small, white, round, a scored line down the middle, no stamp, no letters, no maker's mark. I've taken pills for headaches and they all have tiny codes pressed into them — a letter, a number, so a pharmacist or a poison line can identify them. Mine have nothing. Blank both sides but for the score. Anonymous by design. A pill that doesn't want to be looked up.

I know what you're thinking, journal, because I'm thinking it: don't take it. Skip one. See what the night does without it.

I thought about that all afternoon. And here's the thing that stopped me, the thing that's actually worth the entry:

I couldn't picture skipping it.

Not "I decided not to." Couldn't PICTURE it. I tried to run the movie in my head — me, at eight a.m., not taking the pill — and the film wouldn't play. It skidded, the way my eyes skid off the old journal pages, the way the mail dissolves at nine seconds. The IDEA of skipping the pill hits the same wall as the mail and the past.

Which tells me something enormous, and I'm going to write it plain and then turn off the light:

The pill isn't just protected. Skipping the pill is protected. They didn't only build a wall around the mail and the mother-shaped mysteries. They built a wall around the ONE ACTION that could bring the wall down. The prisoner can't picture reaching for the key, because someone metered the reaching.

But I noticed the wall. That's new. You can't climb a wall you can't see, and I see this one now — a clean skid every time I aim my mind at that eight a.m. moment.

I'm going to keep aiming at it. Every day. Just aiming. Wearing a groove the other direction.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 27 — DR. WHO?

Day 27.

I went looking for the doctor today. The one from when I was eleven. The one who prescribed the journal I'm writing in and the pill I can't skip and, as far as I can tell, the entire architecture of my life.

Here's everything I have of her, dredged up and laid out:

A voice. Warm, unhurried, the kind of voice that makes you want to do well. A manner — kind, patient, pleased with me when I got things right, and I remember wanting to get things right, wanting it badly, the way you want it with an adult who seems delighted by you when so few are.

A room. Cool. Quiet in that same cotton way this house is quiet, now that I think about it — sound-dead, padded. Machines, maybe. A sense of being measured. "It's just a test." Somebody said that to me. In the room. "It's just a test," and I believed it, because why wouldn't an eleven-year-old believe a warm voice.

And that's it. That's everything.

No face.

I sat with that and felt the floor tilt, because I'd been so busy hunting the doctor that I almost missed the obvious: she's a shoes-not-face person. Same as the paperboy. Same category. A whole important human being, reduced in my memory to a voice and a warmth and a padded room, with the face — the one thing that would let me RECOGNIZE her, point at her, name her — cleanly, surgically absent.

The pattern isn't random and it isn't about strangers. It's about anyone who could be identified. The paperboy, who I could pick out of a crowd if I had his face. The doctor, who I could find if I had her face. Both filed down to un-pointable nubs. Shoes. Voices. Warmth. Everything except the part that would let me say THAT ONE. THAT'S WHO DID THIS.

I searched the house for a name to replace the missing face. Records, anything. No medical files, no appointment cards, no insurance paper, nothing — a house buried in red-stamped bills has not one single document about the medical treatment that defines me. Erased, or never filed, or filed somewhere with a PO box and a Bible verse for a name.

The only thing I found was in the front matter of an old textbook of grandma's, a phone number in the margin in grandma's steady secret hand — the upright one, the not-drunk one — with no name beside it. I tried to call it. Phone's still dead. Cut for nonpayment. Of course.

I copied the number into the ledger. Ten digits, guarding a face I'm not allowed to keep.

Somebody sat across from an eleven-year-old, was kind to her, tested her, was pleased with her, and installed the machine. And then made sure the eleven-year-old could never, ever describe them.

You don't erase your own face from a child unless you already know what you're going to do to her.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 28 — THE SLEEP STUDY

Day 28.

A memory surfaced today, whole, unbidden, and it came with a smell, which is how I know it's real and not a thing I built. Made-up memories don't smell. This one smelled like cold and antiseptic and, under that, something scorched — a hot-metal, burnt-hair smell that turned my stomach at eleven and turned it again today, four years later, standing in the kitchen when it hit me out of nowhere.

What surfaced:

A cold room. The padded quiet. I'm eleven. I'm lying down — not a bed, a table, a firm table with a paper cover that crackles. There are lights above me, the big kind, the kind that swing on arms. There's the warm-voiced doctor somewhere near my head, saying the soothing things, and there's the smell, the scorched smell, coming from behind me where I can't see.

And then time does the thing. The thing from the song I don't remember writing but apparently live inside — the melting-clock thing. Time goes soft and long and wrong. A minute stretches into a hallway. The doctor's warm voice slows down and drops in pitch like a record dragging. And I'm not scared, because they gave me something, I can feel that now looking back, I was floating, I was CALM in the administered way, the calm that arrives from outside.

"You did so well," the voice said, from very far away, from the end of the stretched hallway of the minute. "You did so well."

And that's where it cuts. That's all of it.

They called it a sleep study. I know that phrase is attached to this, the way you know a caption belongs to a photo — "the sleep study," said lightly, by grandma, once, years ago, and never again. A sleep study. For a healthy eleven-year-old. In a padded room. On a table. With swinging lights and a scorched-metal smell and something administered to keep me floating while the warm voice told me I did well.

That's not a sleep study. I don't know what it is. But I know what it isn't, and it isn't anyone studying my sleep.

I went to the bathroom after the memory faded and stood at the mirror for a while, just breathing, and that's when I found the last piece, and I almost wish I hadn't:

There's a scar behind my left ear. Small. Old. Pale.
