Right where the hairline hides it, right where you'd never look, right where fingers would go if fingers went looking for a thing put UNDER the skin. I've had it my whole life, I guess, the way you have things your whole life and never see them because they live in the one place your eyes can't reach without a second mirror.

I got the second mirror. I looked.

It's not a scar from falling. It's too clean. It's too PLACED.

Something went in there. When I was eleven. In the cold room. While the warm voice told me I did so well.

I don't want to write the next sentence but a true ledger writes the next sentence:

I think the fog has a source, and I think I've been carrying it behind my ear my whole remembered life, and I think the reason I can't picture skipping the pill is that the pill and the thing behind my ear are talking to each other over my head, and neither of them has ever once asked me anything.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 29 — THE SCAR

Day 29.

I asked grandma about the scar.

I'd decided not to — decided the van and the "they come early" meant I should keep my discoveries off my face, out of the house, quiet. But the scar changed the math. The scar is ON me. It's not a document I can hide in a ledger. It's in my skin, behind my ear, and I've been walking around wearing it in front of grandma for fifteen years, and she has to know, because grandma washed my hair when I was small, grandma has seen behind my ear ten thousand times.

So this morning, sober-window, I sat down next to her on the couch — I never sit on the couch, the couch is hers — and I turned my head and pulled my hair back and I said, "Grandma. What's this."

She looked. She didn't have to lean in or squint. She knew exactly where to look, which told me everything before she said a word: her eyes went straight to it, no searching, a woman checking a mark she's checked before.

And her whole face changed. Not the shop-face this time — the shop-face is a shutter, controlled, chosen. This was something coming apart. Her chin did a thing. Her eyes filled and didn't spill, held at the brim the way she holds everything at the brim.

She reached out and — she almost touched it. Her hand came up, that cracked old hand, and hovered by my ear, trembling, close enough that I felt the warmth off her fingers, and she didn't touch it. Pulled the hand back like the scar was hot.

And she said: "You fell."

That's it. Two words. "You fell." And her voice broke clean down the middle on the second word, on FELL, a two-word lie delivered by a woman falling apart in exactly the place where the lie lives.

It's the worst lie I've ever heard. It's so bad it's almost a gift. Because a good lie tells you nothing — a good lie you have to work past. But a lie THIS bad, delivered THIS wrecked, is a confession wearing a paper mask. "You fell" means: I know exactly what this is, I know exactly what happened, I was there or near it or I signed for it, and I have spent fifteen years unable to say the true thing, so I say the child's thing, the scraped-knee thing, "you fell," and I break in half saying it because we both know you didn't.

I didn't push. I couldn't. She was already reaching for the bottle and it wasn't even ten in the morning.

I just said, "Okay, Grandma." And I let her have it. The lie. The bottle. The couch.

Because here's the thing the ledger made me understand, sitting there watching her hand shake toward the Crown Royal at 9:50 in the morning: she's not lying to fool me.

She's lying because the true thing would bring them early. She's protecting me with a bad lie the same way she protects me with a bottle and a spot on the couch by the window. Everything she does that looks like weakness is a wall she's building with the only bricks they let her have.

"You fell." I love her. God, I love her. What did they make her sign.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 30 — BLACKOUT II

Day 30.

The power went again tonight. A month in this journal, and the lights have died twice; that's the actual rhythm of this house, red envelope to darkness to red envelope, a tide I've stopped pretending is random.

And I did what I did last time, because it's ritual now, because I have questions now: candles lit, grandma blanketed, and me on the warm kitchen floor in the dark, listening for the hum.

There it was. The fridge, running in a house with no power. Faithful. Cold. Paid up.

But tonight I came prepared, because the ledger's made me methodical, and I had a question I've been saving for exactly this dark. Last time I asked the fridge who pays its bill and got nothing, because you can't interview a hum. This time I brought a flashlight and I went behind it.

I pulled the fridge out from the wall — quietly, an inch at a time, grandma's a light sleeper even drunk — and I put the flashlight on the back of it, on the cord, on where the cord goes.

The fridge's own plug hangs loose. Dangling. Not in any outlet. It hasn't been plugged into the wall in — the dust says years. The prongs are dull with it.

And the fridge is running.

One more thing from behind the fridge, and I'm entering it in the flat voice because the flat voice is the only one that will hold it: while I was down there with the flashlight, cheek against the wall, the kitchen window sat in the corner of my eye — black, candle-lit, holding the room the way dark glass does. And the room it held had one person too many in it. Me, crouched, flashlight. Grandma's blanket-shape on the couch through the doorway. And at the table, seated, facing my direction with its hands arranged flat on the wood like a guest who has been told the food is coming: white. Festive is the word my head supplied, and I hate my head for it. It did not move. It was WAITING, and it was delighted to wait, and the delight was the worst part — patient the way a party is patient before the birthday girl walks in.

I stood up into the room and the room was empty. The window held me and the candles and nine mugs' worth of nobody. I pushed the fridge back against the wall, dust and all, and I did not check the glass again, and that — the not-checking — is the first thing fear has ever talked me out of in this ledger.

There's a second cord. Thinner. It comes out of the bottom of the fridge, not the back, and it goes DOWN, into a hole drilled in the floor, into the dark under the house, into wherever the maintained circuit lives — the separate line, the one on someone else's account, the one that pays. The fridge isn't on our power at all. It never was. That's why the blackouts never touch it. It's on THEIR power. It has always been on their power. My whole life the cold in my kitchen has come from somewhere under the floor that answers to somebody else.

I sat down on the floor behind the fridge with the flashlight and I looked at that thin cord going down into the dark and I asked it the question again, out loud, because who's going to hear me, the house is dark and the answer is under the floor:

"Who pays for you?"

And the tank light, all the way across the dark kitchen, clicked on.

By itself. In a house with no power. On the same current, I understand now, as the cold — a circuit that runs when everything else is dead, feeding the two things that watch me. The fish that hears everything. The cold that keeps the pills. Lit and humming in a blackout, on a line that comes up through the floor from someone who never misses a payment on me.

I didn't move for a long time. The tank light glowed. The fridge hummed. Two small faithful machines, awake in the dark, keeping their watch on a girl on the floor.

I'm not scared of them. That's the thing I keep having to write. I'm not scared of the fridge or the fish. They didn't choose. They're just wired to whoever did.

I want to find the panel where that second circuit starts. I want to find the box under this house where somebody's power comes in. I think that box has an address on it. I think the address is the beginning of everything.

The lights came back at 10:40. I plugged nothing back in. It was never unplugged.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 31 — TWO ALWAYS

Day 31.

Tonight I tried to write one sentence and the sentence fought back. That's the entry. That's the whole horror of today: a sentence.

Setup. I've assembled the pieces over a month, and any idiot can see they're pieces of one thing: the pills that never miss. The fridge on its secret power. The circuit under the floor. The account with a verse for a name. Every piece is in this ledger, each on its own page, each written down fine, no trouble, ink flowing like ink.

So tonight I sat down to do the obvious next thing, the thing any investigator does with pieces: put them in one sentence. Connect them. Write the conclusion.

The sentence I planned was simple. Something like: the pills and the fridge are paid for by the same someone, and that someone is—

Attempt one: my pen wrote "The pills and the fridge are paid for by the same" and then drew a long flat line to the edge of the page. I have no memory of the line. I came back rinsing a glass. There is now a small city of rinsed glasses by our sink; archaeologists will wonder.

Attempt two, reading my planned sentence out loud first, then writing fast: got as far as "same someone" — a personal best — then found myself at the window, checking the street, which is worse than the sink, because checking the street isn't a nothing-habit. Checking the street is a JOB. Someone dispatched me to the window mid-sentence like a guard who heard a noise.

Attempt three through six: variations. Wrote it backwards. Wrote it as a question. Wrote it in the third person — "she believes the pills and the fridge share an account" — and third person got one word further, which is a data point I'll be chewing on for days. The wall is slightly less interested in "she" than in "I."

Here is the finding, and it changes the shape of everything:

The nine-second meter doesn't just ration what goes IN. It rations what comes OUT. I can hold the whole conclusion in my head — I'm holding it now, it's clear as a bell in here — but the moment I aim it at paper, at permanence, at EVIDENCE, the governor trips. Reading the mail is metered. Writing the truth is metered. The wall isn't around the information.

The wall is around the assembly.

They don't care what pieces I collect. Collect away, little archivist. They care whether the pieces ever touch.

So tonight I did the only thing left. I wrote the two halves on two separate pages — "the pills and the fridge" on one, "share one keeper" on the other, each half boring enough to slip the meter — and between the two pages I pressed the taped fingerprints from the guest's plate.

The sentence exists now. It's just stored in pieces, like everything else in this house.

Assembly required. I'm working on it.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 32 — REREAD

Day 32.

Today I declared war on my own book.

The facts: this journal is fat. Years fat. And I've established — Day 12, the warm-paper night — that my eyes skid off the old pages, that my hand ribbon-marks to today like a trained horse. Back then I filed it as one more oddity. Now I know better. Now I know a skid is a wall, and a wall means something's behind it, and there is no wall in this house I'm not going to at least kick.

So: systematic assault. Six attempts, documented.

Attempt one, cold open, page one, direct approach. Result: came to at today's page, ribbon in hand, forty minutes gone by the stove clock. FORTY MINUTES. The mail takes nine seconds. My own past took forty minutes of me and left no receipt. Whatever's in the early pages is either very protected or very large.

Attempt two, random access. Opened to the middle with my eyes shut, jammed my finger on a line, opened my eyes. Got: paper. I mean my eyes delivered PAPER — texture, whiteness, the fact of a page — and refused the writing on it. Like looking at a word in a language you almost remember while someone hums in your ear.

Attempt three, one-word rations. If the meter counts intake, take less. Look at a single word, look away. Result: got words! Actual words. "Tuesday." "Kettle." "Him." Individually clean. Then I tried to hold three in a row and the row dissolved, and I understood the meter isn't counting words. It's counting MEANING. Nouns are free. Sentences cost.

Attempt four, grandma read to me. I know. Desperate. I brought her the journal in the sober window and asked her to read any old page aloud. She looked at the book like I'd handed her something dead, and said — sober, steady, terrible: "That's yours, baby. That one's all yours." And would not touch it. WOULD not. Her hands went under the blanket. My grandmother, who signs a stranger's name in a smooth hand, would not put her fingers on my journal.

Attempt five: read it in the kettle's chrome, warped and backwards. Got further than anything — half a sentence, mirrored — before the skid. Filing that. Underlining it. The wall reads left to right. The wall reads FORWARD. Backwards is a crack.

Attempt six: exhaustion. Fell asleep at the desk on the open book and dreamed of the long table, and woke at 2 a.m. with my cheek on a page and one line printed backwards on my skin in transferred ink, readable in the bathroom mirror:

"she's starting to read. good. slow down. they check the ribbon."

They check the ribbon.

I moved the ribbon back to yesterday before bed. Let them find the horse in its stall.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 33 — THE SKIP

Day 33.

Grandma's church has a rummage room, and the rummage room, two years ago, produced a camcorder — the old kind, tape and all, that I got for a school thing and forgot. No internet required. No account. No cloud. Just glass and tape and truth.

Tonight I filmed myself reading the journal.

Method: camcorder on the shelf, angled at the desk, red light taped over (instinct — I didn't examine the instinct, I just obeyed it). Me at the desk, journal open to the deep pages, task: read page one aloud until stopped. Then watch the tape. Simple. A camera doesn't skid. A camera has no meter. Whatever happens in my forty lost minutes, the tape would hold it the way the beach holds the tide.

I have now watched the tape four times. Here is what's on it.

Minute one: a girl reads aloud. Normal. Halting — the words fight her — but normal, a girl doing homework.

Minute one, second forty: the girl stops mid-word. Not trails off. STOPS, clean, like a radio switched. And her face — I've been avoiding this sentence all night — her face goes on standby. There's no other way to write it. The eyes stay open. The person leaves. What remains sits with perfect posture, perfectly still, hands flat on the open book like a pianist waiting for a conductor.

Second fifty-five: the hands begin to turn pages. Fast. Not reading-fast — SORTING fast, precise, a filing clerk's hands, flick flick flick, dozens of pages, and here's the detail that took me until viewing three to catch: the hands pause, three times, at three specific pages. Touch a line. Move on. Checking things. My hands, on standby, checking three things in a book my eyes aren't allowed to read.

Minute three and change: the ribbon goes back to today's page. The posture softens. The girl returns — you can see it arrive, the ME of me, refilling the face like water finding its level — and she picks up the pen and writes, and on the tape you can hear her mutter, in my voice, small and bored: "where was I."

Where was I. That's the question, isn't it. Thirty seconds of somebody, three minutes of something.

I watched it a fourth time for the room instead of the girl. That's when I saw the window.

Behind the desk, in the black glass of the night window, in the far corner of the frame, there is a pale smear. It's small. It could be a reflection of the hall light. It could be lens flare on old rummage glass. It arrives at second forty — the standby second — and it's gone when the girl comes back, and in between, for three minutes, it doesn't flicker like a light or drift like a flare.

It stands there. Roughly the height of a person. Roughly the patience of one.

The tape is labeled and hidden inside the box spring. Evidence now has a shelf.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 34 — UPSIDE DOWN

Day 34.

Breakthrough. Actual breakthrough, and it happened because I got angry, which the ledger should note as a method.

I was at the desk with the forbidden pages and a month of walls behind me — the mail wall, the sentence wall, the skid, the standby girl on the tape — and something in me just snapped sideways. Not despair. Mockery. I got MOCKING, at myself, at my broken head, and in full self-taunting pantomime I flipped the journal upside down and held it up to the mirror like a kid making fun of homework: fine, brain, is THIS how you want it? You want it backwards AND upside down? Would that be dumb enough for you?

And I read a sentence.

A whole one. Upside down, mirrored, my eyes crawling over it like a bug on a wall — and it went IN, and it STAYED, and no skid came, and I stood there in front of the mirror shaking, holding my own diary inverted like a lunatic, having just committed the first successful burglary of my own past.

I'm going to write out what I understand about why, because understanding is ammunition:

The wall reads the way I was taught to read. Left to right, right side up, fluent — the smooth trained gulp of meaning that literate people do without noticing. That's what the meter sits on. That's the pipeline it counts. But upside down and mirrored, there IS no pipeline. Every word has to be picked apart letter by letter, spelled, rebuilt by hand. It's not reading anymore. It's TRANSCRIPTION. It goes in through a different door — slow, manual, ugly — and the meter, the beautiful efficient meter, is watching the front entrance while I climb through the coal chute.

My whole life, this head of mine has done things wrong. Sideways. I count stairs without meaning to. I talk to fish. I read cereal boxes backwards at breakfast for something to do — I have ALWAYS done that, since I was small, and grandma used to watch me do it with a look I never understood and am beginning to understand now.

Every wrong thing about me is a door they forgot to guard. And — entered, because an unguarded door needs a reason — I think I know why it stays unguarded: the sideways habits predate the war. I've read cereal boxes backwards since I was six; the stair-counting, the mirror-games — all of it sits on my baseline, logged for years by whatever logs me under HOW SHE IS. Gauges alarm on change, not on character. They taught their own instruments to ignore the exact thing that frees me.

It's slow. God, it's slow — one sentence cost me four minutes and a headache like a spike. At this rate, reading my own past will take the rest of my childhood.

Fine. I've got nothing but nights, and the night shift already stole those, so we'll call it even.

I'm not writing the sentence I read tonight. Not yet. It deserves its own page, and my hands need to stop shaking first, and some part of me — the part that checks the street, maybe, the part that knows what the ribbon-checkers look for — wants it held one more day, off the page, safe in the coal chute of me.

Tomorrow. The sentence. I promise.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 35 — THE SENTENCE

Day 35.

The sentence. As promised. Copied here the slow way, letter by letter, from a page dated two years ago, in my handwriting, from a day I cannot visit:

"He knocked again today. I wish I could keep him."

That's all of it. Eleven words, two years old, mine.

I've been sitting with it since last night and I keep coming apart in a new place each time, so let me just take it in pieces, ledger-style, before I lose the nerve.

"He knocked." Knocked. Present habit, two years ago — the knock was already old news when this was written. Two soft and one firm. It has to be him. There's no other knocker in my life; there's barely another HE. Two years ago, minimum, this was already happening: him arriving, me answering, the warmth, the nice minute. Two years of nice minutes and I have — what. Green laces. A fraying eyelet. The sound of a laugh with no face on it.

"Again." AGAIN. The word doing so much quiet work. Again means a count was being kept. Again means the girl writing this knew the knocks accumulated, felt them accumulate, lived — two years ago — inside the same daily arithmetic I only discovered a month ago. She knew. I knew. Whoever holds the pen at that hour knew, and filed it in pages I'm not allowed to visit, and I've been meeting him for the first time every single day since.

"I wish I could keep him."

Keep him. Not "keep seeing him." Not "know him better." KEEP him — the way you keep a thing that keeps being taken. You only wish to keep something when you've already learned, over and over, exactly how it gets lost. She wrote that wish two years ago knowing what tomorrow's version of me would do to him: file him under shoes. Wipe the nice minute down to warmth. Lose the name in four seconds flat.

She wished it anyway. Wrote it down anyway, in the protected pages, in the one vault available — a wish pressed like a flower where the meter doesn't reach, where I'd only find it if I ever learned to climb through my own coal chute.

Here's the part I can barely write:

Somebody in me has been loving him carefully, for years, in a place I couldn't see. Keeping the count of knocks. Grieving the daily loss of him ON MY BEHALF, and filing the grief where it would be safe, the way grandma files the cream envelopes, the way this whole house files everything precious — down deep, under the cushions, behind the wall, for later, for someone, for me.

I always thought the fog took things FROM me.

It never occurred to me that something in the fog was also keeping things FOR me.

Tomorrow at 11:40 he'll knock, two soft and one firm, and I'll open the door and it'll be the first time again. But it won't. It hasn't been the first time in years. Someone in this body remembers him.

I wish I could keep him too. There. Now we've both written it.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 36 — MIRROR PRACTICE

Day 36.

Training log. Three sessions today. My head hurts like a dropped melon and I've never been prouder of it.

The method has a name now — I'm calling it coal-chuting, and the drill is this: journal upside down, bathroom mirror, letter by letter, transcribe onto scrap, burn the scrap after memorizing. (Burning is new as of today. The ribbon-checkers taught me that. If they check the ribbon, they check the desk. Nothing readable stays readable in this house except what was already theirs.)

Session one, morning, eleven minutes before the headache won. Yield, from a page about eighteen months back:

"Grandma cried at the sink and said the name again. I pretended to be asleep. The name is not for me yet."

The name. THE name. Said at the sink, by grandma, crying — recently enough that some version of me heard it and had the discipline, the terrible discipline, to pretend sleep and file it away with a note: not for me YET. There's a schedule even for names in this house. Everything on a schedule. Everything rationed, even by the people trying to feed me.

Session two, afternoon, nine minutes.Before session two, an incident, and I'm logging it as an incident because that's the only container that will take it: the drill needs the mirror. That's the method — journal inverted, glass doing the flipping, my eyes doing the crawl. Which means for whole minutes at a time I am face to face with myself at close range, and today, mid-line, my reflection finished a smile I had not started. I want to be precise. I wasn't smiling. I was concentrating, mouth slack, lips moving over a hard letter — and the girl in the glass had a smile arriving, small, at the corners first, the way a curtain lifts before the draft reaches you. It held for one full second. Sixty coal-chute letters long, that second. Then it left, not the way expressions leave a face but the way an actor leaves a stage — done, exit, next scene — and the glass gave me back my own slack concentrating mouth, one beat late, like a call across a canyon.

Here's the trap, and I understood it standing there with my scalp crawling: I can't stop using the mirror. The mirror IS the method. The one door into my own stolen pages has that girl standing in it, and if I want my past I have to keep walking toward her, session after session, letter by letter, and she knows it, if smears know things, and I think this one does.

 Yield, from somewhere deeper, the writing smaller, more tired:

"Practice went long. My hands know the whole board now. I hate that I'm good at it."

I have no idea what board. We own no board. Piano, chess, switch — no. But I read "my hands know the whole board" and my knuckles ACHED, right then, sympathetically, the deep fine ache from the residue ledger, the ache of doing something small and exact thousands of times. Whatever the night job is, it has a board, and my hands have learned it, and the version of me who wrote this HATES being good at it — hates it the specific way you hate a skill someone forced on you and then praised you for.

You did so well. That's what the warm voice said in the cold room. You did so well.

Session three, tonight, six minutes, headache arriving early like the van. Yield — and this one I copied twice to be sure, because it's not like the others, it's not a report, it's addressed:

"If you're reading this the slow way: good. The slow way is ours. Don't rush. They notice speed."

Ours.

Not "mine." Not "yours." OURS. Whoever writes in the deep pages knows about the coal chute — of course she does, she built it, she's been leaving flowers pressed in it for years — and she's watching my progress, and she says WE.

Three sentences today. Three days of someone else's life. My life. Ours.

I burned the scraps in the sink and washed the ash down and stood there gripping the counter while the tap ran, doing the thing I do now instead of crying, which is inventory: I have a partner. I have always had a partner. The partner keeps what I lose, loves what I forget, hates what they've made us good at, and left me a coal chute and a trail of pressed flowers because she knew — she KNEW — I'd come climbing eventually.

Don't rush. They notice speed.

Okay, partner. Slow it is.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 37 — GRANDMA'S JOURNAL?

Day 37.

I did a bad thing today, and then I undid it, and I'm not sure which of the two I regret.

The logic that started it was clean enough: this house files its treasures in the dark. The cushions eat cream envelopes. My journal's deep pages hold pressed flowers. So when grandma took her bath — the long Sunday kind, the door locked, the radio on, forty reliable minutes — I did what any investigator with a month of walls behind her would do.

I searched her room.

I'm not going to pretty it up. I went through my grandmother's room the way the standby girl goes through my journal — fast, sorting, a filing clerk with a deadline. Closet: clothes, mothballs, the dressed-nice outfit hanging apart from the others in a garment bag, recently cleaned. Dresser: what dressers hold. Nightstand: reading glasses she claims she's lost, a church program dated years ago, gone soft at the folds from handling.

And under the bed, pushed back against the wall where the shadow lives: a box.

Metal, old, olive-colored, the kind that survives things. A hasp with a small padlock — a real lock, not a suggestion. Heavy when I slid it out; contents that shifted with a papery, layered slither. Documents. A box of documents under my grandmother's bed, locked, in the house where nothing is written down and everything is filed in the dark.

I sat on her floor with the radio muffled through the wall and my hands on the lid, and every investigative cell in me said: this is it. The clause. The name. The cream envelopes' final resting place. The one thing she kept. Whatever runs this house on its secret circuit, the paper trail is under the bed, and the bath has thirty more minutes on it, and the lock is small, and I know where the toolbox is.

And I put it back.

Slid it into its shadow, matched the angle — and I want to record precisely why, because it wasn't nerve and it wasn't goodness:

It was her hands at the sink, crying, saying the name that's not for me yet.

She has a schedule. She's been running it for fifteen years at a cost I'm only starting to itemize — the bottle, the couch, the watch at the window, the practiced signature, the two-word lies that break her in half. Everything she does is rationed toward some finish line only she can see. If I crack the box early, I don't just steal documents. I steal her one thing — the timing — the single lever they left her.

So the box stays locked. For now. That's a loan, grandma, not a gift.

One observation, though, filed before I left the room, because the ledger takes what it takes:

Everything under that bed wears years of dust. The floor, the wall, an old shoe, a dead moth.

The box is clean.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 38 — THE DUST

Day 38.

The clean box has been eating at me, so today I built a dust map, and the dust map broke my heart, and I need to write it down in order or it won't hold still.

A clean box in a dusty underworld means one thing: traffic. The box gets visited. Opened, handled, returned — often enough that dust never settles, careful enough that nothing around it is disturbed. My grandmother, who misses coasters, runs a clean-hands operation under her own bed.

So: when? I've lived in this house my whole life. When does she visit the box?

I did what the ledger's taught me — stopped guessing, started charting. Pulled a month of observations into one page. Her sober windows. The bath schedule. The Thursday vans. The bottle tides. And the thing I'd noticed on Day 21 and filed as one more grandma-mystery: once a month, she dresses nice. The garment-bag outfit. Shoes with heels she can barely manage. She leaves in the early afternoon — I've never once been invited, never once thought to follow, and tonight, charting it, I finally heard how strange that is: I never THOUGHT to follow. A month of walls has taught me what it means when a thought reliably fails to occur. That's not absentmindedness. That's a fence.

The dressed-nice day was four days ago. I checked my ledger against the box.

I'd searched her room three days ago. The box was clean THEN — freshly clean, wiped-clean, visited-within-days clean.

She dresses nice. She visits the box. Then she goes somewhere, for three hours, and comes back — and here is the detail I've watched a dozen times without seeing — she comes back drunk AND composed. Not couch-drunk. Ceremony-drunk. Lipstick still on. Eyes wrecked. The specific state of a woman who drank to get through something formal and succeeded.

Once a month, my grandmother opens a locked box, puts on her best clothes, and goes to conduct a ceremony she has never mentioned, at a location the fence in my head won't let me wonder about, and comes home looking like a widow leaving a courthouse.

And the next morning — I checked the tide chart — the next morning is always, ALWAYS, the month's worst. Bottle open before nine. Four sentences down to two. The couch a life raft.

Whatever the ceremony is, it costs her a month of strength every month, and she pays it, and has paid it, on schedule, for as long as my records reach.

I keep thinking about the wall at night. The pleading through the plaster. I kept the one thing you asked. Please let it have been enough.

She's not reporting to THEM. You don't dress nice for your jailers.

She's reporting to someone she owes. Someone she answers to out of something worse than fear.

Next month, on the dressed-nice day, I'm going to follow her. The fence can file its objection. I'm going.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 39 — PAGE COUNT PREP

Day 39.

It started as a joke tonight. I want that on the record: the biggest thing I've found yet, and I found it clowning around.

I was at the desk after coal-chute practice, head aching, and I picked up the journal to put it away, and it just — it's HEAVY. Have I ever said that plainly? This book is heavy. It's a hardback ledger-style book, and it sits in the hand like a brick, and tonight, punchy from the headache, I held it up and said out loud to the fish, "Four years of nothing sure weighs a lot."

And then I stopped, because the sentence had a splinter in it.

Four years. That's the number I always say. Journal since I was eleven, doctor's orders, four years. And the book is heavy, and the book is THICK, and I have skidded off its depths for a month, and it has never once occurred to me — the fence, I know the feel of the fence now — never ONCE occurred to me to ask the stupidest, most obvious question in the world:

How thick is four years?

So I did math. Weir-style, back of a scrap, burn after reading. Say I write a page a day. I don't — I know I don't, I skip days, everyone skips days — but call it a page a day, the absolute ceiling. Four years, page a day: call it fourteen hundred and change. Fourteen hundred pages. That's three fat novels. That's — I went and got the fattest book in the house, an old dictionary — that's about one and a half dictionaries.

Then I measured my journal against the dictionary.

I'm not going to write the ratio tonight. Partly because my hands are cold. Mostly because I don't trust one measurement, and the ledger has rules now: no conclusion stands on one leg. Paper thickness varies. Bindings lie. Maybe ledger paper is thin, maybe there are blank pages padding the back, maybe the manufacturer — maybe, maybe, maybe. I have a whole page of maybes. Every one of them is load-bearing and every one of them is creaking.

Because here's what I can write, the observation that needs no ruler:

I held the dictionary in one hand and my four alleged years in the other, and my arms knew the answer before the math did. My arms have carried this book to the desk every night, felt it grow the way you feel a child grow — not at all, and then suddenly, and then you refuse the sudden because the refusing is easier.

There's a fence around the thickness. There has ALWAYS been a fence around the thickness. And a fence, I've learned, is the surest sign in this house that something on the other side is worth the lumber.

Tomorrow I count the pages. All of them. By hand, in the coal-chute way, slow, one by one, past whatever fences turn up.

Tomorrow I find out how long I've actually been in here.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 40 — ARC FINALE: THE COUNT

Day 40.

I counted the pages.

It took the whole day, and I did it right, because I knew — some part of me knew — that whatever number came out, I'd spend the rest of my life needing to trust it. So: method first, like always, like armor.

I counted in fifties, upside down, in the bathroom mirror — the coal-chute way, so the wall couldn't skid me — with a tally scrap and a system: fifty pages, a mark, re-square the stack, again. When the fence pushed, and it pushed — twice I found myself at the sink with a clean glass and a lost quarter hour — I went back to the last mark and recounted the fifty. No skipped batches. No trust. Every mark earned twice.

Grandma watched me carry the journal and the tally into the bathroom for the sixth time and said nothing, which I understand now. She's watched me walk toward this number before. I'll get to that.

The count finished at 4:50 in the afternoon, in the bathroom, in the mirror, by candle stub because the light in there died last week and we don't fix things.

I'm going to write the arithmetic the way I wrote it on the scrap, because the scrap is ash now and this page is the only place the number gets to live:

My journal, at one entry a day, every single day, no misses — and I now believe there are no misses; I believe attendance is mandatory in a way I never chose — holds not four years.

It holds more than ten.

Ten years. And change. Change I couldn't finish counting because my hands stopped being steady enough to trust the tally.

I am fifteen. The journal began, they told me, when I was eleven. Four years. The doctor with no face said anchor yourself in the day, and I have anchored, apparently, every day, for over a decade, in a book I was handed four years ago. The pages in my two hands tonight outnumber the days I have been alive since that cold room. They may outnumber — I can't hold this thought flat, it keeps tipping — they may outnumber the days I've been alive at all.

What the mirror gave me at mark thirty — the thing my forward eyes have skidded past at this desk every night of my life. Re-squaring the stack between counts, watching my hands work in the glass, I saw that the stack was stacks. Seams. Spines. The "journal" I carry to the desk each night — one book, one weight, one object, I'd have sworn on anything — sits in the mirror as volumes: five, six, uniform, the newest on top, the older ones beneath wearing the exact dust-shadow of a desk they have never left. I turned around: one book. I looked back into the glass: volumes. My forward eyes deliver the object I'm supposed to own — continuity is cheap to fake, and nobody audits her own bookshelf. Sideways, the instruction doesn't parse, and the desk tells the truth: I own a shelf I have never seen. The maybes are all dead. Every volume full, every page dated, every hand mine, the same broken-loop g, going down and down and down into years that cannot fit inside me.

Here is the only sentence I have, and it's tonight's whole finding, and I wrote it in the mirror first to make sure it was true:

I have been keeping this journal longer than I have been me.

The candle's almost out. The house is cotton-quiet. Somewhere under the floor, the circuit hums, faithful as ever, and for the first time I think I finally understand what it's been keeping cold all these years.

Not the pills.

The evidence. Which means somewhere in this house there's a cold, humming box that has been keeping the proof of me fresh for longer than I've been allowed to know I'm the kind of thing that needs proving. I should feel triumphant. I found it. Instead I keep thinking: they kept the receipt. For four years, they kept the receipt on a girl they were spending.

Goodnight, again. Whoever you are tonight — goodnight, again.



############################################################
ARC 2: THE CIRCUS (Episodes 41–80)
############################################################

============================================================
EPISODE 41 — THE FIRST FRAGMENT

Day 41.

Something got through today. From the other side. From the night.

I was at the sink — it's always the sink, I think the sink is a weak spot in the wall, all that repetition, all those rinsed glasses have worn the plaster thin — and I was doing nothing, hands in warm water, and the kitchen went away.

Not fainting. I want to be precise, because precision is the only tool that still works. I didn't lose time; I gained a place. For maybe ten seconds I was somewhere else while my body stayed at the sink, and the somewhere else was so vivid it made the kitchen look like the dream.

Here's what I saw, before it's gone, and it's already going, they go so fast:

A clock. On a wall. Melting — sagging off its nail like it was made of wax, the numbers sliding down toward the floor in a long slow drool. And I knew, in the fragment, that this was normal. That the clock always did this. That time was a thing that dripped here.

And a smell. God, the smell. The scorched one. The one from the cold room, the sleep-study smell, hot metal and something burnt — but stronger, closer, all around me, the smell of the place itself. And under the scorch, fainter, a smell I knew even better, a smell I couldn't place and couldn't shake, warm and animal and close.

It smelled like me. That's the closest I can get. The place smelled like the scorch and, underneath, like ME — like it was made partly out of me, like I'd been breathing in that room so long the room had learned my smell and given it back.

Then the water was too hot and I was at the sink and the kitchen slammed back into place so hard I dropped the glass. It broke. I stood in the shards not moving.

I've been writing "the fog takes my nights" for forty days like the nights were a blank. They're not blank. They were never blank. There's a PLACE in there, with a melting clock and a scorched smell and my own scent baked into the walls, and today a crack opened and I saw ten seconds of it, and every cell in me recognized it.

You can't recognize a blank.

I've been going there every night of my life. Today's the first day I remembered the door.

New ledger, its own section, first entry. I'm calling it FRAGMENTS. Time, trigger, content, and a column I've never needed before: DID IT FEEL NEW OR OLD.

Tonight's answer, in the last column, in a hand that shook: old. So old. Older than anything.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 42 — CANDLELIGHT

Day 42.

Second fragment. Longer. And it ended with me standing somewhere I didn't walk to, holding something I didn't pick up, so the wall between the place and the kitchen is getting thin in both directions now. Things are starting to come through WITH me.

It came at dusk this time, not the sink — the threshold between the kitchen and the hall, that spot where the light changes, and I've noted before that thresholds are thin. I stepped through the doorway and the hall wasn't the hall.

It was a table. A long one. Longer than any table has a right to be, running off into a dark I couldn't see the end of. Set — beautifully, formally, endlessly — places marching down both sides into the black, plates and silver and folded cloth, dozens of them, hundreds maybe, a banquet laid for a crowd that hadn't come. Candlelight. Everywhere, warm and low, throwing long shadows off the silver.

And at the far end of the table, where the dark started, someone was seated.

I couldn't see them. Just the shape — upright, patient, at the head of the table, waiting the way you wait for a meal you've ordered. Not moving. Not menacing, exactly. Just there, at the far end of the long table, in a chair like a small throne, watching the empty places between us.

And I understood, the way you understand things in the place — completely, wordlessly, as fact — that one of those places was mine. That I was expected. That the seated shape had laid all of this and was prepared to wait as long as it took for me to come and sit.

Then dusk was just dusk, and I was standing in my own hallway, and there was a candle in my hand.

A real one. A taper, half-burned, wick warm, the smoke-ghost still rising off it. The same candle — I know it's the same candle — from the set table on Day 22, the one grandma went gray over, the one I thought I'd put in a drawer.

I put it down on the hall table like it was hot. Then I picked it back up, because evidence, and I carried it in here, and it's sitting next to the journal as I write, this small ordinary object that keeps crossing over from the banquet in my head to the drawer in my house and back.

The set table isn't a symptom. That's what I understand tonight. The set table in my kitchen is a piece of the long table in the dark, LEAKING — the night place bleeding through, one place setting at a time, into my mornings. Every candlelit two-top I've found is the banquet trying to reach the kitchen. Or someone at the banquet, setting a smaller table, closer, closer, meeting me halfway.

Fork and knife in candlelight. I keep thinking that phrase and I don't know where it's from. It sounds like a lyric. It sounds like mine.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 43 — THE RULES OF IT

Day 43.

Enough being haunted. Time to be a scientist about being haunted.

I've got fragments coming now — three in three days, counting a small one this morning (a wheel, turning, and the sense of running very fast while standing still) — and the old instinct is to cower. I'm overriding it. A phenomenon that recurs can be studied. A phenomenon that can be studied can be understood. A phenomenon that can be understood can, eventually, be USED. That's the whole ladder, and I'm putting my foot on the first rung tonight.

The FRAGMENTS ledger, formalized. Every entry gets: time, trigger, duration (I'm estimating in breaths now, since clocks lie in this house anyway), content, and the new column — HYPOTHESIS. (Full disclosure: I also spent forty minutes today with a rescued magazine about celebrity kitchens. Marble everywhere. No secret cords, presumably. The investigation resumed refreshed.)

I'll log what I've got and hypothesize as I go, because a scientist reasons on the page.

Triggers so far: the sink (repetition, warm water, hands busy). The dusk threshold (light change). This morning, the moment right after the pill (interesting — the pill's supposed to WALL the night, and the fragment came right on its heels, like a wave sneaking in behind the door as it closes).

Content so far: the melting clock (time is wrong there). The scorched smell + my own smell (the place is partly made of me). The long table + the seated watcher (I'm expected). The wheel (I run without moving there).

First hypothesis, the coward's one, the one I have to write to cross it off: I'm losing my mind. Ordinary teenage psychosis, stress, a sad house, a sad girl, hallucinations. — Rejecting it. Not out of pride. Out of DATA. Psychosis doesn't leave physical candles in your hand. Psychosis doesn't ache your knuckles on schedule. Psychosis isn't corroborated by a decade of journal pages and a tape with a standing shape in the glass. My mind is doing a lot of things, but it isn't manufacturing this from nothing. The evidence has weight and warmth and dust.

Second hypothesis, the one that's been standing behind the first one waiting to be let in:

My mind isn't losing ME. Someone is losing my mind FOR me. On purpose. Nightly. And the fragments aren't a breakdown — they're a breakDOWN, a wall coming down, the night place becoming visible because whatever holds it shut is finally, slightly, failing.

The pill's failing. Or I'm outgrowing its dose. Or the coal-chuting — all that sideways reading, all that climbing through the unguarded door — has worn a groove the night can follow back up.

I did something and the wall got thinner. That's the hypothesis. And if I DID something, then I can do MORE of it, on purpose, and see the whole table instead of ten seconds of it.

That's tomorrow's experiment. Tonight's just the ladder.

Foot on the first rung. Up we go.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 44 — MACHINERY OF LIGHT

Day 44.

Today's fragment showed me my own hands, and I don't recognize what they know how to do, and I can't stop looking at them now — they're on the pen right now, writing this, and they scare me.

It came mid-afternoon, no clear trigger, just a slip — the kitchen guttered like a candle and I was there instead.

Above me: light. Machinery of it. Not lamps — I don't have words for this, so I'll build them. Panels of light, banks of it, hanging and curved, the swinging-arm kind from the cold room but MORE, a whole ceiling of them, and they weren't for seeing by. They were for working IN. The light was the workspace. The light was the thing I was reaching into.

And my hands were reaching into it. Fast. Certain. Sorting — that word again, the tape word — moving through the light the way the standby girl moves through the journal pages, flick, flick, pause, a thing selected, flick, moving on. Skilled. My hands were so skilled. They moved with the awful fluency of a thing done ten thousand times, a fluency I have never earned at anything in my waking life, where I can barely make toast without narrating it.

I watched my own hands do expert work in a language of light I've never studied, and the residue-ache started RIGHT THEN, in the fragment, the deep fine knuckle-ache blooming as I watched, cause and effect finally in the same frame — THIS is what aches them, this reaching, this sorting, this endless exact selection in the banks of light.

And there was a sound. Under everything. A voice.

A woman's voice. Level, unhurried, endless. Reading. Reading numbers — no, reading a LIST, item after item, steady as a metronome, and I couldn't make out the items but I could feel their weight, feel that each one MATTERED, that the list was the point, that my sorting hands and her reading voice were doing one job together, her naming and me — choosing? finding? — and I didn't want to know the verb, in the fragment I already didn't want to know the verb.

Then the kitchen, and my hands flat on the counter, aching, and me staring at them like they belonged to someone I'd never met.

Because they do. They belong to her. To the night one. To the version whose knuckles know the whole board, who hates being good at it, who does expert work in banks of light to the sound of a woman reading a list that matters.

I keep my hands in my lap now when I'm not using them. It doesn't help. They're always right there, at the ends of my arms, knowing things I don't, waiting for eight a.m. and the little white gate and the dark on the other side of it where the light-work is.

The residue ledger and the fragment ledger just shook hands tonight. Throat: I scream. Legs: I go somewhere. Hands: I work in the light. It's one job with three costs.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 45 — THE FIGURE

Day 45.

I named it tonight. I've been circling for four days and tonight I put the word on the page, and putting the word on the page made it real, and I did it anyway, because a thing you won't name is a thing you can't fight.

The seated figure. The watcher at the head of the long table. The pale shape in the tape's window glass. The white smear seated in the kettle's chrome. It's all one. I've been logging them as separate anomalies and they were never separate, they're one recurring presence, and tonight in a fragment I saw it closer than the far end of the table.

It stood up.

That's what happened today. The table again, the candlelight, the endless places — but this time the figure at the head rose from its little throne, and came a few steps down the table's side toward me, into the reach of the nearest candles, and the candlelight caught it.

White. A white face. Not pale skin — PAINT, thick and matte and cracked at the creases like old plaster, white greasepaint over a face, and around the eyes and mouth the paint gave way to color, to a drawn-on expression that didn't match the eyes inside it. The eyes inside it were tired. The painted face grinned. The real eyes, in the holes of the grin, were exhausted and kind and terribly patient, and they were fixed on me.

And then it behaved, and the behavior was worse than the face. It laughed — one laugh, a single dry bark — a full beat AFTER nothing happened. Not at me. At a joke told on some other frequency, at a punchline the room owed it. Then it looked down at the place setting nearest me, saw the fork lying a half-inch out of true, and corrected it — slowly, with two fingers, with CEREMONY, the way you'd straighten a photograph of someone you'd lost — and bowed its head over the fork for a moment as if the fork had feelings that required acknowledgment. And then it pulled out the chair. Not for itself.

And it said a name. The voice came out wrong-sized, too small for the paint, a voice from a music box left in the rain, and the name it said was not my name. It was the OTHER one. The name from the envelopes. The fake name, the bills-of-shame name, the name of the girl the mail keeps insisting lives here — it offered the chair to HER, to the misprint, to the girl on paper, and it waited, beaming at the empty air beside me, host of a party thrown for a clerical error.

I understood then that whatever this is, it does not run on sense. It runs on procedure. It is following steps from a program I can't see, at a tempo I can't hear, and it has me confused with my own paperwork — or it doesn't, and that's worse, and I'm not writing the second option twice.

A clown.

There. That's the word. It's a clown. It has been a clown this whole time — the thing that sets my table, that waits at the banquet, that stands in the black glass while my body files pages. A painted white face with a grin that isn't a grin, sitting at the head of a table set for a guest who is me.

I know exactly how insane it is to be a fifteen-year-old writing "there is a clown in my nights" in her diary. I know what that sounds like. I know the ledger is supposed to be cold. But the ledger's whole job is to write down what's true even when what's true is a clown, so: it's a clown, and I'm not laughing, because clowns wear a face over their face, a fixed feeling painted on so you can't read the real one — and I of all people, I who lose every real face I meet and keep only the masks, I understand exactly what it means to be a thing whose true expression has been painted over.

It didn't come closer than a few steps. It didn't need to. It just wanted, I think, to be seen. To be named. Forty-five days I've been investigating my nights and tonight the thing at the center of them stood up and walked into the light so I'd finally have to write the word.

Fine. Written. It's a clown.

And here's the part I'll be turning over till morning: standing in the candlelight, grinning its painted grin, watching me with its tired real eyes — it didn't feel like a predator.

It felt like it was waiting for me to recognize it.

That's the part I can't put down. Not that a painted thing wants to eat me. That something built it patient. Something taught a piece of me to hold that smile for years, sit still at that table, and call it a shift.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 46 — FRONT ROW

Day 46.

I need to write about the watcher behind the watcher. Not the clown. Me. The part of me that watches me.

Today's fragment: the tent. A big top, striped canvas soaring into dark, rows of empty seats curving away. And in the fragment I wasn't just THERE — I was watching myself be there. Some cool removed part of me sat in the front row of my own experience, taking notes, narrating: interesting, the seats are empty, log that. The canvas smells like the scorch. Log that.

And the floor tilted, because this watcher is not new. This watcher is in every entry of this whole journal. It's the cold one. The ledger-keeper. The part that drops the glass and starts cataloguing the shards instead of feeling the fear. Forty-six days I've praised her — thank God for the cold part, she takes notes, she doesn't panic — and tonight I finally asked the obvious question:

Who is she? Why do I have a full-time internal narrator with perfect composure while the rest of me falls apart?

People don't come standard with this. Regular fear doesn't have a stenographer. But I've had one my whole life — a permanent front-row observer who watches the show of me and writes it all down and never, ever flinches. Like she's seen it before. Like she's seen it so many times that flinching stopped being worth the energy.

I'm the circus, I wrote in the fragment ledger tonight, not knowing why. Front row seat. It fit so well, and I don't know where it came from, and it sounds like a lyric, like a line from a song I've never heard and somehow wrote.

Except — and I only connected this tonight, mid-sentence — there IS a tune. The no-source tune. The one I hum at the sink without deciding to, the one that made grandma go statue-still one evening years ago, mid-dry, dish in hand, and leave the room without a word. I have words with no song and a song with no words, and tonight is the first time I've wondered whether they're each other's missing half.

Here's the thing I have to end on, because it happened as I was writing THIS — this entry, this page:

The handwriting changed.

One word. Up above, where I wrote "she takes notes" — look at it, if you're reading this. Look at the word "notes." The N is wrong. The slant is wrong. The pressure is wrong — grooved deep, braced back, leaning against a wind I wasn't writing in. For one word my hand wasn't mine.

I've read it forty times. I've put the lamp at every angle.

It's still wrong.

It's 11:12. I'm going to bed with the light on.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 47 — THE WRONG WORD

Day 47.

I spent today with a magnifying glass and a wrong word, and I came out the other side with a hypothesis so big I can barely fit it on the page, so I'm going to build up to it slow, the way you approach a wall you finally understand is a door.

The word is "notes," from last night. The tilted one. I got grandma's magnifier from the drawer of things that don't work but we keep anyway, and I put it over that word, and I compared it to every other "notes" and "note" in the journal's recent pages, all the ones I KNOW I wrote.

Findings, forensic, cold:

Same pen. Obviously — one pen, one desk. Same ink. Same paper. But the HAND is different in a way I can now name precisely, because I've stared at it for hours. My "n" starts with a little upstroke, a running-start hook, because I was taught cursive by a grandma who was taught it by nuns. This "n" starts cold — no hook, straight down, a print-influenced stroke, the hand of someone who learned to write differently or later or under different orders. My letters lean forward, eager. This word leans BACK, braced, like it's writing against a wind.

And the pressure. Mine is light — I barely touch the page, I always have, teachers complained. This word is pressed HARD, grooved into the paper, dented three sheets deep. I checked. Three sheets. You can feel the word "notes" on page forty-seven with your fingertips on page fifty.

That's the same detail as the notes. The Day 1 notes, "sleep again," "good talk" — I never checked, but I'm betting my whole ledger they're grooved too. Pressed hard. Braced. Leaning back.

So here's the wall that's a door, here's the hypothesis I've been building toward since a candle showed up in my hand:

First, a name — because the ledger can't keep writing "the other hand," and she earned one the day I found her tell: G. For the broken-loop g that gives her away in every line she's ever touched. G, then. And here is the hypothesis about G:

G isn't somewhere else. G isn't in the mirror or the tank or the night place, reaching me across a gap. There's no gap. G is HERE. In this hand. This exact hand at the end of my exact arm. The notes aren't delivered from outside — they're written from INSIDE, by someone who uses my body when I'm not using it, who holds my pen with a heavier grip and a colder hook and a backward brace, because she learned to write under conditions I don't remember and can't imagine.

One body. One pen. Two hands' worth of habits sharing it.

The front-row watcher, the cold colleague, the night one, G — same person. Not haunting me. INHABITING me. Or — and this is the version that makes my chest hurt — not inhabiting me. Being me, the other half of me, the half that's awake when I'm switched off, doing the light-work, setting the table, keeping the count of knocks, loving the boy I forget, and leaving me notes in a pressed-hard hand because it's the only mail that crosses the wall between her shift and mine.

I don't have proof yet. I have a magnifier and a tilted word and a feeling like the ground opening.

But I know how to test it now. And the test frightens me more than anything I've planned, and I can't do it yet, because the test is: skip the pill, and meet her.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 48 — TANK GLASS

Day 48.

I've stopped looking at anything that reflects. I want to explain why, and then I want to explain why it doesn't help.

It started with the kettle, Day 15, the white smear seated in the chrome. I turned the kettle to the wall and told myself it was a trick of light. Then the tape, Day 33, the pale shape in the black window during my lost minutes. I told myself: lens flare, rummage glass. Then last night I walked past the goldfish tank.

The tank glass, when the hall light's behind you, throws a dim reflection — the hallway, the doorway, a slice of kitchen. I glanced at it in passing the way you glance at any glass, and in the reflected kitchen, at the reflected table, in a chair that in the real room is empty:

White. Seated. Patient.

The clown. Not in a fragment. Not in my nights. In the TANK GLASS, in the reflection of my waking kitchen, at four in the afternoon, sitting at my table watching the back of my head while I fed the fish.

I spun around. Empty chair. Afternoon light. The fish doing his lap, unbothered, and why would he be bothered, he's on their circuit, he's one of the watchers himself.

I turned back to the tank. Gone. Just hallway and doorway and a girl with a pinch of flakes, alone.

So now I don't look at reflective surfaces. The kettle faces the wall. I've draped a dish towel over the worst part of the tank glass. I keep my eyes down passing the hall mirror. It's a whole choreography now, a not-looking dance, and grandma watches me do it and says nothing, and I think grandma has done this dance. I think grandma's been not-looking at reflections for fifteen years. It would explain a lot about a woman who lives in a house and never once checks her hair.

Here's why it doesn't help, though. Here's tonight's actual finding:

The reason it's in the reflections and not the room is that reflections are BACKWARDS. And I know what backwards means now. Backwards is the coal chute. Backwards is the unguarded door. The wall that skids my eyes off the mail and the past — it works on the forward world, the direct look, the trained fluent gulp. But a reflection is already reversed, already sideways, already through the door the wall doesn't watch.

The clown isn't hiding in the reflections. The clown is APPEARING in the only place the wall lets it through. It's using the coal chute too. Same crack I climb to read my past, it climbs to reach my present. We're both squeezing through the one unguarded door, from opposite sides, toward each other.

Covering the mirrors doesn't lock it out.

It locks US out. From each other. And something wants us covered. Something would rather I spend my life terrified of the one face reaching for mine than let the two of us stand in the same glass for a single unwatched second.

Tomorrow I'm taking the towel off the tank.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 49 — IT'S JUST A TEST

Day 49.

A voice spoke to me today. Awake. In the hall. And I heard it with my ears, not in a fragment, and I've spent all night trying to decide if that's the wall breaking or the wall speaking, and I still don't know.

Dusk again — the thin hour, the threshold hour. I was standing in the kitchen doorway (on purpose now; I stand in thresholds on purpose now, fishing) and the light was doing its shift, warm to gray, and out of the seam of it, quiet, close, right at my ear, a voice said:

"It's just a test."

That's all. Four words. And then dusk was dusk and the hall was empty and my heart was going like a fist on a door.

Here's what wrecked me about it. Not the fear — I'm getting used to fear, fear's an old roommate now. It was the RECOGNITION. I knew that voice. Not "it sounded familiar." I KNEW it, the way you know your own name called across a room — or a smell from childhood, before you can place it. That voice has said those words to me before. Many times. "It's just a test." Warm. Reassuring. The voice you'd trust. The voice you'd want to do well for.

The cold room. When I was eleven. "It's just a test," the warm doctor said, and I believed her, because why wouldn't you believe a kind voice in a soft room when you're eleven and floating.

But it wasn't the doctor's voice today. That's the thing. It was CLOSE to it — the same words, the same reassurance — but younger, and rougher, and nearer, coming from inside the house instead of across a desk. Like someone was doing an impression of the doctor. Or like someone had heard the doctor say it so many times, to me, over me, that they'd learned to say it back — to soothe me the way I was soothed, with the exact phrase that was used to put me under.

G. It was G. Saying the doctor's line. And I can read two things into that and both are true and they contradict and I'm keeping both:

One — it's cruel. She's mocking the phrase they used to trap us, throwing it back at the dusk. It's just a test. Sure it is.

Two — it's tender. It's the only lullaby she has. The doctor's voice is the voice that soothed us into this, and it's ALSO, horribly, the last kind voice either of us heard before the dark closed in, and so when G wants to reach across and calm me down, that's the phrase she reaches for, because it's the only calming phrase we were ever given. She soothes me with the poison because the poison is all the medicine we've got.

It's just a test.

I said it back to the empty hall. Quiet. Both of us mocking it and meaning it at once.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 50 — YOU NEVER LEFT

Day 50.

Fifty days. Halfway to nowhere, or halfway to her; I can't tell which yet. Today the mirror answered a question I asked out loud, and the answer has four words, and I've written them on my hand so I don't lose them to the morning.

I took the towel off the tank, like I promised. And I went further — I stood in front of the hall mirror, the one I've been not-looking at for two days, and I made myself look, direct, into my own reflected face, and I asked it a question. Out loud. Feeling like a fool, feeling like a priest, feeling like a girl at the bottom of a well calling up.

I asked: "Are you in there?"

And the reflection — my own face, my own mouth, I was watching my own mouth — my reflection's lips moved a half-beat out of sync with the silence, the way a badly dubbed film drifts, and I heard, quiet, in that near rough younger voice:

"I never left."

I want to take those four words apart the way I take everything apart, because taking things apart is how I keep my hands from shaking, except tonight it isn't working, so bear with me.

"I never left." Not "I'm here." Not "yes." LEFT — a word about departure, about a leaving that could have happened and didn't. She's answering a bigger question than the one I asked. I asked are you in there and she answered a question from years ago, a question maybe I asked at eleven, in the cold room, going under: are you going to leave me? And the answer, saved all this time, delivered fifty days into my investigation through the surface of a hall mirror: I never left. I never left you. Through all of it, every night, every wiped morning, every forgotten face — I never once left.

And there's the other reading, colder, and I have to write it because a true ledger writes the cold one too:

"You never left." Maybe that's what she said. Maybe I misheard I for you. And "you never left" means something terrible — means I'm still there, still in the cold room, still on the table, that the girl who went in at eleven never came out, that everything since has been the dream of a girl who never left the chair. The mirror swears I never left. Which of us never left? Her, staying with me? Or me, still under, still eleven, still floating in the scorched-smelling dark while a warm voice says I did so well?

I've written both on my hand. Left hand: I NEVER LEFT (her). Right hand: YOU NEVER LEFT (me). I'll wash one off in the morning depending on which one I can bear.

Fifty days. I have a partner in the mirror who never left, or a self in the dark who never left, or both, because in this house it's always both.

Goodnight, again. I never left either. Whichever way you meant it — me too.

============================================================
EPISODE 51 — THE SAME SCRIPT

Day 51.

I've started transcribing the voices, and the transcription broke the case open in a way I didn't expect, because the voices have a vocabulary, and the vocabulary is SMALL.

Here is every distinct sentence the voices have ever said to me, compiled from fifty days of ledger, fragments, thresholds, and mirrors:

"It's just a test."
"I never left." (Or "you never left." Both filed.)
"You always ask that."
"Good talk."
"Not yet."

Five sentences. I went back through everything — every dusk visitation, every mirror drift, every note — and it's five sentences, recombined, re-inflected, but five. The shadow doesn't improvise. The mirror doesn't chat. Whatever reaches me from the other side reaches me with the same five phrases, over and over, like a traveler who memorized five sentences of a foreign language before the trip.

And that's the finding. That's exactly what it is.

Think about what crossing over must cost. The notes come through pressed three sheets deep — effort, force, a hand braced against wind. The mirror-voice drifts out of sync like a signal barely holding. Whatever wall stands between her shift and mine, getting ANYTHING across it is expensive, and when something is expensive you don't waste it on new material. You send the phrases you've tested. The ones you know arrive intact. The ones that have survived the crossing before.

Five sentences aren't a script because she's limited. They're a script because they're PROVEN. Each one is a route she's smuggled words through successfully — worn paths across the wall, and she keeps to the paths because the paths are the only places the wall is already broken.

Which reframes every one of them. "It's just a test" isn't just an echo of the doctor — it's a phrase the wall PERMITS, because the wall was built to let the doctor's phrases through. She's mailing me letters inside the system's own envelopes. Using their stamps. Their approved language, hollowed out and refilled with her meaning, because contraband travels best inside official packaging.

The wall reads the words and waves them through. I read the words and hear her.

That's not a haunting. That's tradecraft.

So tonight I did the reciprocal thing, the thing that took me embarrassingly long to think of: I picked one of the five, and I said it to the dusk, deliberately, as a message OUT. I stood in the threshold at the thin hour and said, clear and slow:

"Good talk."

Nothing answered. The hall stayed a hall.

But when I came back to the desk, this journal was warm, and the pen had been moved two inches left, and I am choosing to log that as: message received.

We have five envelopes between us. Time to learn to write smaller.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 52 — INTERVIEW WITH A SHADOW

Day 52.

I conducted a formal interview with a shadow today. Prepared questions and everything. I'm logging the transcript in full, including the silences, because the silences turned out to be the testimony.

Setting: the kitchen threshold, dusk, the thin ten minutes. Me, standing, notebook in hand like a reporter. The hall going gray. Grandma on the couch pretending to sleep — pretending; her breathing was wrong; she was listening; log that too.

INTERVIEW TRANSCRIPT:

Q: "Who are you?"
A: (nothing)

Q: "What do you want?"
A: (nothing)

Q: "Are you the one who writes the notes?"
A: (nothing)

Q: "Are you the clown?"
A: (nothing — but the hall light flickered once, and I've decided not to build a religion on a flicker, but log it, log everything)

Q: "What happens at night?"
A: (nothing)

Q: "What's behind my ear?"
A: (nothing, and the silence after this one had a different texture — I know how that sounds; silences don't have textures; this one did. It went tight. The dusk held its breath.)

Q: "Am I in danger?"
A: (nothing)

And then I gave up. That's important to the record: I'd asked my seven prepared questions into a gray hallway like a lunatic, gotten one flicker and one tight silence, and I closed the notebook and said, not as a question, just tired, just to myself:

"Are you even real?"

And the dusk said: "You always ask that."

Immediately. No lag. The way you answer a question you've been waiting for through six wrong ones.

I stood very still and understood several things at once, and I've been sorting them since:

One. She won't answer new questions. Can't, maybe — new answers would need new words, and new words don't survive the crossing. Five envelopes. My prepared questions were asking her to write outside the envelopes, and she let them fall.

Two. "You always ask that" was true TWICE. It answered the surface question — am I real, you always ask that, you've asked it your whole life, in mirrors, in the dark, at eleven in a cold room. And it answered something underneath: it told me these interviews have happened before. That some prior version of this investigation, some prior ME, stood in a threshold with prepared questions. That I am walking a walked path. That she has been patiently receiving this exact interview, and answering with her five envelopes, for longer than my forty-page memory can hold.

Three — and this is the one I'll sleep on — she answered the question that HURT. Not the tactical ones, not the ones about danger and notes and ears. The one that came out small and tired and human. She spends her expensive words on the moments I stop being an investigator and start being a girl.

She's not a witness. She's family.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 53 — TRIANGULATION

Day 53.

Map day. I took everything — every note, every voice, every fragment, every anomaly in fifty-three days of ledger — and I plotted it all on one page, by time of day, looking for the shape of the thing. The shape is there. I found the coastline.

The data, compressed:

The NOTES arrive overnight, discovered on waking. Always.
The FRAGMENTS hit at the sink (mid-morning, repetitive task), the threshold (dusk), and once right after the pill (8 a.m.).
The VOICES speak at dusk, and once from a mirror at night.
The TABLE gets set in the deep night.
The WARM JOURNAL: evenings, always evenings, always just before I sit down to write.
The lost time, the standby girl, the sorting hands: night, and the drowsy edges of afternoon.

Plot all of that on a clock face and the shape jumps out at you the way a coastline jumps out of soundings: everything — every single contact, every anomaly, every leak — clusters at the BORDERS of sleep. Falling toward it. Climbing out of it. The drowsy shoulders of the day. Dusk, which is the world falling asleep. Dawn's edge, which is the world waking. The sink-trance, which is the mind idling at the gate.

Nothing ever happens at high noon with my eyes wide open and my attention lit. Nothing ever happens when I'm sharp. The other side of me can only reach across when I'm within sight of the border — when the two territories, waking and sleeping, share a checkpoint.

So the wall isn't a wall. That's the wrong shape and I've been using the wrong shape for weeks. It's a BORDER — with a strip of contested ground on either side, a no-man's-land of dusk and drowse where the fences thin and things can be passed hand to hand between the two countries of me. She lives in the night country. I live in the day country. Neither of us can cross. But we can both, with effort, at cost, reach into the strip.

Everything she's ever gotten to me — every note, every phrase, every candle — came through the strip. Everything I've ever gotten back — the warm journal after I spoke to the dusk — went through the strip.

Which means the strategy writes itself, and it's so simple and so dangerous that my hands went cold putting it on the page:

If I want more contact, I need more border. More time in the strip. More drowse, more dusk, more of the drifting in-between — ON PURPOSE. Fish the gray hours. Widen the checkpoint.

And the far end of that strategy, the logical terminus that I can see from here like a lighthouse I'm not ready to sail to:

The widest the border ever gets is the moment of crossing itself. The falling-asleep moment. The one the pill slams shut every single night at the border's widest point.

The pill isn't a wall either. It's a border guard. And it's posted at the exact spot where we'd otherwise meet.

Goodnight, again.

============================================================
EPISODE 54 — THE DUSK HOUR

Day 54.

I fished the strip tonight. Deliberately, methodically, for the whole width of dusk — and I caught something, and grandma caught me fishing, and the second thing matters more.

Method: I found the exact minutes. Our hall light has a warm-up — old wiring, it comes on gray-pink and takes a while to remember yellow — and the voices live in that warm-up. So tonight at dusk I stationed myself in the threshold, flipped the switch, and stood in the changing light with my mind idling in neutral, drowse-adjacent, the way you hold your eyes soft to see a faint star.

The light warmed. The seam opened. And out of it, quiet, close:

"Not yet."

Just that. Envelope number five. But here's what was new — the TONE. I've had "not yet" once before, in ink, and ink has no tone. Tonight it had one, and the tone was strained. Effortful. The sound of someone saying two words while carrying something heavy — and underneath the strain, unmistakably, direction. Not "not yet" as in be patient. "Not yet" as in I know what you're planning, I can feel you circling the pill from your side of the border, and NOT YET — wait — there's a right time and this isn't it and I am spending my whole day's postage on holding you back.

She knows. Of course she knows. We share a hand and a house and a head; my plans are held in the same skull she works in. She's read my ledger. She's watched me map the border. And her first use of tone, her first inflected word in fifty-four days, is a hand on my chest: not yet.

I said back, to the seam, out loud: "Then when?"

Nothing. New question. No envelope for it. The light finished warming and the hall was just the hall.

And when I turned around, grandma was standing in the kitchen doorway watching me.

Not couch-watching. STANDING. Upright, gripping the frame, and her face — I've catalogued grandma's faces, I have the shop-face and the gray face and the calculation face — this was none of them. This was recognition. She was looking at me standing in a threshold at dusk talking to a light seam, and she was not confused, and she was not surprised, and she was not curious.
