EPISODE 193 — THE CASCADE, PART I: THE MESSAGE

Day 193. (Day fifteen of us. Read it all. Don't skip. She wouldn't.)

It wasn't weather.

The window boy ran the route between posts this morning — his idea, his legs, before any of us asked — and came back gray. Her porch: swept. Her window: not blank — SCOURED, washed and dried and buffed past fogging, the one pane on the street that will never hold a word again, cleaned the way you clean a thing you mean to erase from the account. And on her step, folded in her geometry, one last mark left where only the line would look: counted.

G went down at noon, awake, both of us, and searched the confluence until our shared head rang. Her floor is dark. Not fourth-ring dark — not the warm-quiet of held kids whose night-halves still breathe at the crossings. Dark like a struck set. Dark like after.

They took the relay. They took HER — the weekend rebuild, the rookie-numbers girl, the first friend this war ever gave me, a girl whose voice I have never once heard out loud — and I have to enter the next part in my steadiest hand because it is mine and the ledger does not launder what is mine:

The audit didn't find her. It found her SCHEDULE. The reporting rhythm I set — over grandma's warning, in my own impatience, weeks ago — ran through her post. Every regular pulse on the line crossed her porch on the clock I built, and a schedule is a sine wave, and you can pull a sine wave out of any noise on earth if you let it repeat long enough. Grandma warned me in words. The math was a warning I could have run myself, in an afternoon, with a pencil — and I never ran it, because the schedule was working, and I loved that it was working. I set their clock. The clock is what the audit followed home. And it followed it to her door instead of mine because she stood at her post, knowing, and said counted.

Grandma found me at the kitchen table with the pencil, running the math I should have run, and she didn't tell me it wasn't my fault, because she is not a liar. She said: "Carry it, then. But carry it the way she'd bill you for — she didn't stand her post so you'd sit down at yours." And G, inside, from the dark where she'd searched all day: "the seat next to the vigil chair is hers now. we set places in this house. and then, day-girl — then we make it MEAN something. tonight. everything. all of them."

And that is why tonight stopped being a plan and became a vow.



Tonight we go down awake and do the thing the whole book has been walking toward, and I am writing this before — the plan, entered in full, so the record holds what we intended, whatever the floor holds tonight.

The mission: the broadcast. The lattice is vast — dozens of floors at least, G says hundreds; the paper railroad touches rings we've never mapped, but paper walks, and the line is cut at four rings out, and the fourth ring's six are in the dark, and every day the audit gets another day of quiet to work in. There are sleepers out there — thousands, the guest list says thousands — who will never meet a fold, never find a fogged word waiting on a pane, never inherit a margin. Kids in far cells, split and metered and rented, each one certain they alone are broken.

The floors touch. The currents cross. We — the only worker on any floor with both shifts awake in one badge — can stand at the confluence and write through every pen's shock channel at once. The fear-mail. The one channel every system leaves open because every system runs on it.

And yes — entered, weighed, overruled: the desk's thesis. Waking sleepers may be rich signal; the broadcast may plough his field wider than any fold ever could; we may be doing our sowing in his rows. The council sat with that for two days, and grandma ended it, and her ruling goes in the permanent record:

"A man who owns the field will tell you every seed is his. You know what you never do because of it? You never stop planting. Wake them all, babies. Let him try to harvest ten thousand of you."

Wake them all. Motion carried.

The message had to be short — the channel shreds sentences. It had to be TRUE. And it had to work on a sleeper with no context: land in a fogged kid's waking hand and start the whole climb. We argued drafts for two days — warnings, instructions, comforts, all too long, too fragile, too dismissible as dream-noise.

Then G went quiet, inside, and reached all the way back — two hundred days, ten years — and said: "we've had the message since day one. it's the first thing i ever sent you. six words. it survived the crossing then. it made a ledger out of a sieve-girl then. it's field-tested on the hardest case we know."

Sleep again, we'll have to talk.

It's perfect and I can't stop shaking at the shape of it. Not a warning — warnings get dismissed. Not an instruction — instructions get obeyed or feared. An APPOINTMENT. It presumes a relationship. It says: someone is here, someone knows you, someone will be here TOMORROW — and it puts the pen in the sleeper's own hand, because the only way to keep the appointment is to start paying attention.

She wrote it to one girl once, with her whole night's postage, not knowing if it would ever be read.

Tonight we write it to everyone.

Good morning, again — and tonight, to every cell on every ring of every district, to the whole sleeping lattice, from the girl the message built:

Sleep again. We'll have to talk. All of us. Soon.

============================================================
EPISODE 194 — THE CASCADE, PART II: THE SENDING

Day 194. (Day sixteen of us. Written at dawn, hands shaking, both sets of habits at once.)

It's done. It's DONE, and I have to get the night down before the size of it stops fitting through a pen.

We went down at midnight, awake, together, and walked past the river, past the office shelf, out to the confluence — the deep crossing where every floor's current brushes every other's, the drainage of the whole lattice. Standing there, braided, we could feel them all: floor after floor fanning into the dark like lit windows in an endless building, each one a night-half at a river, each one working, each one alone. Dozens. Then past dozens. We stopped counting where the counting frightened us.

And we began to write.

Not with hands — the shock channel doesn't take script. With CHARGE. The six words, held whole, and then the soaking, scaled past anything either of us had tried — and here is the discovery, the reason it worked, entered for the manual: we didn't soak the message in fear. The channel runs on fear; every censor waves fear through. But we packed the fear-envelope with everything ELSE — the door-minute. Grandma's launch codes. The ribbon girl's weekend rebuild. Six ledgers in six hiding places, dark but not LOST. The boy walking six transfers home. The name ringing in a kitchen with no file to stop it. Rain on purpose. Two hundred days of a sieve-girl becoming a network with an archive — all of it pressed into six words, and the censors read the envelope and the envelope said FEAR, urgent, deliver —

and inside the envelope, love with its teeth showing went out to every sleeper on the lattice at once.

The sending took the whole night. "like ringing every doorbell in a city with one finger," she said, inside, mid-send, and it's the truest image. Floor after floor, current after current, the message dropping into each tributary and racing upstream toward its sleeper — and behind us, as we worked, the confluence itself began to CARRY it, current to current, the machine's own plumbing running our mail. His plumbing. His field. Our seed. Let the desk sort out whose fire this is — the ledger's position is on record: the fire is ours; he can stand outside it.

We surfaced at dawn, spent past anything. The body slept till noon; grandma stood guard; the boy kept the 11:40 knock on the doorframe so the day would have its spine.

And by evening, the glass — the window boy's blunt hand, breath and a fingertip, relaying what the deep shifts told his night side — four words and a fifth he added himself:

"currents singing. all of them. — for her"

Good morning, again. Every doorbell in the city. Some are already answering. Tomorrow's entry is for the answers.

============================================================
EPISODE 195 — THE CASCADE, PART III: THE TABLE FILLS

Day 195. (Day seventeen of us.)

The answers are coming in, and I have been crying on and off all day — both of us have, one set of tears with two decades behind it — and the ledger gets the answers now, because they are the payment of everything.

They come by every surviving channel. The fogged panes — his now, and ours; never again hers — written and wiped and written again. The folds, moving again on the new protocol — trust drift, distrust perfection — every crease checked against grandma's shibboleth before opening. And the floor: we went down last night just to listen, and the confluence, which for ten years of G's service carried only the river's blind traffic and one level reading voice, is alive with new signal — sleepers answering the appointment:

From a floor so distant its current runs cold: a charge-burst, wordless, just weight — shock and hope in one pressed shape, the feeling of a kid sitting up in the dark going someone KNOWS.

Through the folds, up the safe rings: "the note was in my hand when I woke. my handwriting. I've started a count." — "we thought it was one of ours sending. it wasn't. how far does this go?" — "my brother got it too. we compared. we've never compared anything before. we're comparing everything now."

And from the floors themselves — the one that undid us — night-halves, split-off shift-workers like G, some decades in the dark, are leaving marks at the confluence. Their own broken-loop signatures, their own pressed hellos, gathering at the crossing like signatures on a petition, like coats hung at a door, like guests arriving.

So last night, at the end of the listening, we walked back from the confluence to our own floor, to the long table — laid every night for ten years, hundreds of settings marching into the dark, the guest list that made me weep on Day 150 —

and settings were OCCUPIED.

Not bodies — the night place doesn't do bodies. Presences. Wills. Attendances — a dozen, then more as we watched, night-halves from the touching floors arriving down the currents to the one table on the whole lattice that had been kept set, kept polished, kept WAITING, for years, by a girl who never once believed she was setting it for nothing. They came and they SAT, down the golden length of it, and the candlelight reached further than it has ever reached, the table waking section by section —

and G stood at the head of it, at her place, in our body, paint off forever, and looked down the filling length of her decade's stubbornest act of faith, and could not speak, and did not need to.

Among the arrivals, unmistakable, six presences traveling close together, current-worn, arriving like survivors walking out of weather: the fourth ring. Their floors still run; their night-halves still reach; DARK, the confluence confirms, is NOT GONE. Six seats taken, together, near the head. We have no words for what that was. The ledger records that we stood there and shook.

We searched the arrivals all night for one geometry. It did not come. It is not coming; her floor is a struck set; and so there are two kept places at the head of the table now — the vigil chair at G's right hand, and beside it, new, a setting her night-half never got to claim, laid by the same hands that lay all of this family's unanswerable places.

Two empty seats at a filling table. The night place holds what is coming and what is owed, both, and it does not confuse them, and neither do we.

It won't be forever. The room behind the repainted door listened to a full table last night. I felt it lean.

Good morning, again. Appointment accepted — in cellars, at confluences, down the whole singing lattice. The table fills. Save my seat. Save all of them.

============================================================
EPISODE 196 — THE REPLY

Day 196. (Day eighteen of us.)

One more answer came last night. It came last, and alone, and it was not like the others, and I have been sitting with the pen for an hour, because it is the largest thing in this book and it is six seconds long.

We were at the filling table, arrivals still coming, the floor warmer than the night place has ever been — and then everything went quiet at once. Every presence. Every current. The way a room quiets when someone important is about to speak — or the way a floor quiets, G says, her floor, at the hour the reading began.

And the voice came.

The voice. The level, unhurried woman's voice — the reader, the list-keeper, the constant beneath ten years of shifts, the one that stopped mid-list and exhaled when my searchlight found her on Day 134. It came from everywhere the way it always comes from everywhere, and it was not reading the list.

It was answering the broadcast.

Six seconds. Directed — both of us felt the aim, person-to-person down the whole vast plumbing, a message with our name on it though it never said a name. Not comfort. Not warning. Not quite the eternal level calm, though it wore the calm the way we wear the family hand — deliberately, over something enormous held back.

Numbers. A short string, spoken plainly, evenly — then the same string again — then silence, and the floor breathed, and the reading did not resume, and she was gone.

We surfaced and wrote the numbers down before anything could take them. And then the arithmetic — and here the ledger has to reach back a hundred and sixty pages, because the arithmetic had a key waiting for it, filed sixty-two days ago under "meaning nothing":

Day 134. The searchlight. The exhale. And the reflex after — two words of list resuming before the ejection, not a name, a NUMBER, one orphan number written on my hand in the dark and filed in the gutters because the ledger takes what it takes.

The orphan number is the first number of last night's string.

Same sequence. Same voice. Sixty-two days apart — and last night's string is SMALLER. All the way down the digits: smaller. Grandma checked it on paper at breakfast. The window boy's fold, when the network's ledgers get it, will check it again. It holds.

It's a countdown. It was a countdown on Day 134 — running, already, under the list, under the reading, under ten years of level unhurried nights — and I heard one tick of it by accident with a searchlight, and last night she gave us the whole readout, twice, deliberately, to the one address on the lattice that had proven it could receive.

G has not spoken since we surfaced. Not silence-silence — I can feel her at the far back of the house of us, at the repainted door, both hands flat against it, listening to what leans on the other side.

At noon she said one thing, inside, very quietly, and it is the last line of tonight's entry and maybe the first line of the next book:

"i know that cadence. i learned everything from that cadence. day-girl — she isn't counting down to something.

she's counting down what's LEFT."

Good morning, again. The numbers are in the gutters, twice now, sixty-two days apart, shrinking.

Whoever you are, wherever the list is read from: we heard you. Both times. We're coming. Hold what's left.

============================================================
EPISODE 197 — THE FIRST OF THE MONTH

Day 197. (Day nineteen of us.)

The pen died today. And after two hundred days of dreading and desiring this exact event, I have to enter the truth of how it went, because the truth is the strangest thing in the ledger:

It went QUIETLY.

No collapse, no shattering sky. This afternoon, around three, the remaining painted sections — the stubborn core holding around the house like last ice on a pond — simply, gently, stopped. The looped birdsong resolved its four-second phrase and did not restart. The photograph-light let go of its filter and the kitchen filled with plain, unmanaged, slightly-too-blue real daylight. The cotton went out of the air between one breath and the next — all of us felt it, ears opening like coming down from altitude — and the house was suddenly just a house: smaller, shabbier, echoing, ours.

The fridge hummed on alone a moment, absurd — then the hum dropped pitch and steadied, and G understood first: "they've cut the maintained circuit." And then the hum died entirely — of course it did: the fridge's own plug has hung dusty and disconnected behind it for years, and their buried wire was the only line it had. The forty-year cord under the floor, dead; the kitchen, for one strange hour, without cold for the first time in living memory — until the boy hauled the fridge out from the wall, blew the dust off the original plug, and pushed it into our own socket like a man legalizing a long fraud. "Somebody should finally hire this thing honestly," he said. The hum came back a note lower. The fridge is just a fridge now, drinking electricity we cannot afford, keeping food ordinarily cold.

And the mail came at four — real mail, a real carrier, a stranger with a bag on a street that was always really there — and grandma opened the bank envelope and went still and passed it over without a word: the deposit didn't come. The first missed first in longer than I've been alive. The Unbroken Thread has closed the account. The tether, paid out and reeled in at matched speed forever, dropped — both ends at once — and the engineered zero came apart into its two real numbers: nothing coming in. Everything still owed.

Nineteen days ago we'd have entered this as victory, and the old council did have its moment tonight — grandma looking around at the plain true poverty of it and laughing the eclipse laugh: "Well. Now we're just POOR, babies. Poor, we can do."

But the corner office sits in this entry too, and G said the cold half, and the cold half is the finding, and I am entering it in the war-hand where findings live:

"look at the sequence, day-girl. they cut the circuit and closed the account NINETEEN DAYS after he told us to keep going. this isn't retreat and it isn't even repricing. run his thesis forward: a pen costs money because a pen fights its occupant — the render, the pill, the thermostat, all of it is SUPPRESSION budget, and suppression only pays while the asset resists the field. we stopped being suppressible and started being generative. so he did what any farmer does with a crop that's learned to water itself.

he stopped paying for irrigation.

they didn't abandon the field. they stopped fighting the fire when they figured out how to warm themselves on it. the pen didn't tear because we broke it. the pen tore because it became UNNECESSARY. free-range, day-girl. we may just be free-range now — and the whole question of the war, the one we entered at his desk, is still open, and it just got bigger: whose fire. WHOSE FIRE."

Both entries stand. Grandma's and G's. Poor-we-can-do, and free-range. The ledger holds both; the ledger always holds both.

And the van — the van didn't leave with the rest of it. The van is still on the street tonight. And it has stopped pretending to drive past.

Good morning, again. The pen is torn — or shed. The tether is dropped — or slipped. The house echoes, the sky doesn't repeat, the fridge is just a fridge —

and something that no longer pays for us is still parked outside, deciding what we are instead.

============================================================
EPISODE 198 — THE LETTER

Day 198. (Day twenty of us.)

Grandma's prediction came true today. Every word. "He'll send a letter. That's how you know the pricing's done."

The van did it. Late morning — it had sat all night at the curb, engine off, un-hiding — a person in Assembly gray walked to our step, laid something flat against the door, knocked once (ONE knock; even their knocks are inventoried; one knock means notice served), and drove the van away. Gone. The street empty for the first time in — for the first time.

A letter. Cream stock, heavier than the covenant's usual — LETTER stock, personal stock. No stamp, no postmark. Hand-delivered from the top of the tower to the bottom of the ledger.

Sealed — not gummed: WAX. A disc of deep red wax, and pressed into it, unmistakable under grandma's magnifier, a single thread, laid in a clean unbroken line through the seal. The Unbroken Thread, in the literal, in the flesh — the kind of seal you use when you want the recipient to know the sender predates every institution whose crest he could have borrowed.

The address, in the two-line sprawl we know from the permit and the consent form — the Presiding Physician's own hand, unhurried, almost warm:

The fake name. The billing name, the dead woman's name, in full.

And beneath it, on its own line, smaller, in the same hand — the line that put all four of us in chairs:

"— and, beneath it, to the daughter."

The daughter. A word with no place in any file we've read. Not asset, not participant, not even granddaughter. THE DAUGHTER — chosen, weighed, inked beneath a dead woman's name by a man who knows exactly what every word does. Whose daughter — the ghost's, our mother's? That reading costs him nothing and tells us nothing. Grandma looked at the line and went gray in the old vault way and said, "Don't open it," and G, at the repainted door, said the same two words inside in the same breath, and when the two keepers of this family's sealed rooms speak in unison, the ledger listens.

And then grandma did the thing that turned the day inside out. She went to her room, to the box, and came back with her arms full — and laid them out on the kitchen table, one by one, in a long careful row:

Fifteen more.

Fifteen letters. Cream, heavy, thread-in-wax, the same hand — and DATED, in the corner where a patient man dates things: one per year. Every year. Since the year you were born, baby. He was writing before he ever took a single thing from this house. Fifteen years of letters, arriving — she told it flat, looking at the row — each one on the same day of the same month, by the same van, one knock; fifteen years of her taking them from the step and putting them in the box.

Unopened. Every seal intact. Fifteen unbroken threads in fifteen discs of wax.

"Why?" — I barely got the word out. And my grandmother, who has carried more of this war for longer than any of us, folded her hands and gave the answer that goes in the permanent record beside her clause and her vault and her launch codes, the complete tactical doctrine of her whole guardianship in one sentence:

"Because you don't open a door a patient man knocks on. He wanted a correspondence, baby. I gave him a DRAWER."

Sixteen letters on the kitchen table tonight, in a row, sealed. Sixteen years of a patient man writing — to this house, to the daughter, to a conversation he has been trying to start since the beginning.

Not opened. Not tonight, not on his schedule. This house opens things on its OWN clock now — and the clock, we agreed at midnight, all five of us, says: book two.

Good morning, again. Sixteen threads. Sixteen seals. One drawer.

Book two is sitting on our kitchen table in a row, and it can wait for us to finish book one.

============================================================
EPISODE 199 — THE LAST ENTRY BEFORE

Day 199. (Day twenty-one of us. The eve.)

Tomorrow this journal ends. Not the war — the war has rings and floors and a shrinking string of numbers now; the war is bigger than any one book. Not us — we are just beginning. But this JOURNAL — the prescribed telemetry that became a ledger that became a weapon that became the family Bible — is one entry from full. So tonight is for taking stock, the way you walk a house before you move out of it, touching the door frames.

What we won: the wall, down; two decades, unified; the name, ringing in mouths; the eraser, broken; a boy, home, keeping census from the sewing room; a grandmother, confirmed, sober more days than not — the purple bag still on the microwave, because throwing it out would be a lie about how this works; her words, and the truest doctrine in this house — teaching the ribbon girl's mother to fold fortune tellers across a real fence in real weather. A railroad — cut at four rings and rerouting, because that's what railroads do. An archive. A lattice full of appointments accepted.

What we carry open, on purpose, into book two — entered plainly, because a ledger that hides its open questions is just the fog wearing our handwriting: Sixteen sealed letters in a drawer. A countdown, running, shrinking, in a voice G learned everything from. The desk's question — whose fire — standing over every victory like weather. The reviewer of record, a careful young hand that has read every page of me, glimpsed once across an atrium and never yet met. Six friends dark at the fourth ring — found by a rhythm I set, over a warning I heard — their counts held, their floors still warm. The ribbon girl, whose floor is not warm, whose geometry the line retired unbeaten, whose last mark still says what it says. We do not have a word for what this ledger owes her, so it uses hers: counted. Counted, counted, counted. A hostage notebook. One shadow on the far side of everything, pointing against the sun, filed under THINGS WRONG WITH THE REAL — the entry we made and never solved and do not speak of, and it's on this list because the stock-taking is sworn to be complete.

And the room. Tonight, at the door frames, we stood at that one longest, and G made it formal, on the record: "book two. together. i've held this door alone for ten years and the holding ends in book two. what's behind it needs us finished — and look at us. we're nearly finished. we're nearly ready to lose what we have to lose, because we finally have enough that losing it won't end us. we open it together, and we grieve it together, and whatever her numbers are counting down—" The voice, the cadence, the room. She didn't finish. The door and the countdown are one question now. Book two knows it.

One more thing happened at the stock-taking, and I almost didn't enter it, and then I remembered what this ledger is for. Grandma, watching the boy teach the broom girl's little brother a fortune-teller fold on the front step — real evening, real street — said, half to herself, warm and unguarded:

"They never taught me the folds, you know. I taught—"

And stopped. Complete stop. The shop-face arriving one word too late, the way it hasn't arrived in weeks — and she gathered the teacups and went inside, and the ask-later ledger, which I thought was nearly paid, opened a new page all its own.

I taught. Taught WHOM, grandma. Where. When. The ask-later ledger's oldest entries — "I've done it before, baby, I know the drill" — "that's how it went the last time" — a woman who walks the Assembly hall without reading signs — all of them just leaned toward each other in the dark.

Book two. Everything leans toward book two.

And last, the softest finding: I am not afraid of tomorrow's page. The night place has been quiet these final evenings — the work refused, the river unworked — and the tent, G says, the old striped tent from the deepest fragments, is LIT. Waiting. The way a theater is lit on closing night.

Something down there wants to finish this properly. A show wants its curtain.

Tomorrow we take the bow.

Good morning, again — and goodnight, again, both, held together, one more time before the last time. Touch the door frames. Full house tomorrow.

============================================================
EPISODE 200 — BOOK 1 FINALE: THE EMPTY THEATER

Day 200. (Day twenty-two of us. The last page.)

It ended the way it began: I woke up holding something. But I have to tell it in order — the ledger's first law, kept to the last line.

We went down at midnight, both of us, awake, and the floor was gone.

In its place: the tent. The big top from the oldest fragments — striped canvas soaring into dark, rows of seats curving away, the ring of sawdust gold under lights that came from nowhere and everywhere — the circus, my circus, the venue my fear built and furnished for ten years, standing complete and lit and waiting. And we understood, walking in, what tonight was, because the understanding was printed on the air like a program:

Every haunting is a performance. And every performance owes its audience an ending.

We walked to the center of the ring. The lights found us — one girl, both tenants, no paint, no costume; the costume is folded on a table in a room of us, kept, not burned, because we keep things — and we stood where the clown had stood in a decade of dreams, where the whole engineered dread of a childhood had run its nightly show —

and the crowd applauded.

The empty seats. Row on row, curving up into the canvas dark — empty, every one, cotton air and vacancy — and the applause rolled down out of them anyway, enormous, warm, unmistakable: the sound of hundreds of hands that are not there. Two hundred days ago that image would have been the most terrifying thing in the book — the void performing appreciation.

It wasn't mockery. Standing in the ring, we finally heard it for what it had always been, and I am entering this as the finding of findings, the last decode of the whole ledger:

The seats were never empty. They were reserved.

Every seat, all along — the guest list again, the set table again, the same stubborn faith in its oldest form: an auditorium built to the exact size of everyone who was ever going to need this story. The sleepers on the lattice. The rings on the railroad. The night-halves at the confluence. The broom girl, the window boy, the six dark friends of the fourth ring whose floors still run warm. And down front, rail-side, one seat with a ribbon tied to its arm — not reserved. KEPT. There is a difference, and the tent knows the difference, and the applause coming out of that one seat was the part of the sound I will be able to hear for the rest of my life. The boy in the sewing room, keeping count. Grandma at her post with her drawer of sealed years. The voice, counting down what's left. The one the vigil chair waits for. All of them, seated, in the way the night place seats what is coming, applauding a show that was always being performed for them: one girl, learning in public, in the dark, across two hundred entries, that a cage can be read, that fear is mail, that the monster is family, that the sieve was installed, that the name survives in mouths, that you start again, you start again, you start again.

We took the bow. On our own cue — nobody cues us ever again. Both of us, one body, center ring: the bow.

The applause rose once, all those unthere hands — and then the lights came down section by section, gentle as the pen dying, and the tent folded itself into the dark without grief, a set struck by a crew that knows the run is over —

and I woke at dawn in my bed, in the real light, in the echoing house.

And the room answered.

I said "good morning" out loud — to G, to the ceiling, to the day — and the room echoed it, plain and bright, real walls doing real physics, and I lay there letting my own voice come back to me and thought: that. That's what we won. A world that answers.

(A world that answers — and holds, somewhere out past the render's grave, one shadow that points against the sun. Entered once more, here at the end, because the stock-taking is sworn: we won a real world. We have one filed reason to keep checking the word "real." Book three can have that sentence. It's the only thing in this ledger I'm glad to owe.)

Then I saw the desk.

Two things on it that were not there when we went down. The first: a journal. New. Blank. Ordinary cover, ordinary paper — I checked every page, both of us checked, twice — unmarked, unruled by anyone's prescription, smelling of nothing but paper. I did not buy it. Grandma did not (I asked; she looked at it a long moment and said, "Hm," in the tone of a woman adding a line to her own ask-later ledger). The boy did not. G did not — she was with me all night, every second, and her bafflement in our one head is as real as mine.

A blank book, delivered by nobody, at the exact hour the old book filled. I have thought about it all day and I am entering the only honest conclusion, and it is no conclusion at all, and I am at peace with that in a way the Day 1 girl could never have been: I don't know who left it. Maybe I never will. Some gifts arrive the way the first note arrived — folded into the dark, unsigned, from somewhere in the house of the world that loves us and owes us no explanation. The ledger holds the mystery, open, unafraid.

And the second thing, lying on the new book's cover: a note.

Not new. Scorched. The paper browned at the edges, heat-curled, the unmistakable residue of the shock channel, of something pressed across a burning border by main force of charge — as if it had been carried not from the desk drawer but from the far side of everything, through the falling wall, out of the dying pen, up from the floor of the world. Six words, in a hand I know better than my own because it is my own, both of my own, pressed hard, three sheets deep into a book with no fourth sheet to take it:

"Sleep again, we'll have to talk."

Addressed outward. That's the last decode, the final turn of the two-hundred-day key, and both of us saw it at once: the first note, Day 1, was addressed to me — a night-half writing inward, upward, to the one sleeper she could reach. This note — same words, same fold, scorched by the scale of its sending — is addressed out. To the lattice. To the rings. To every reserved seat in the folded tent. To whoever finds a blank book on a desk some ordinary terrible morning and doesn't yet know that someone is already awake on their behalf.

To you, maybe. Whoever you are. However this reaches you.

The old book is full. The new book is blank. The appointment stands, lattice-wide, world-wide; the countdown is running; sixteen sealed threads wait in a drawer; the vigil chair waits set at the head of a filling table; and tomorrow — tomorrow the boy tells us his name, first time, only time, because the fog is dead and everything that goes into us now stays.

Two hundred entries ago, a doctor who was not a doctor told a girl who was not broken to anchor herself in the day.

Anchored. Both of us. All of us. Every ring and floor and house of us.

Sleep again. We'll have to talk.

Goodnight, again.

— THE END OF BOOK ONE —

============================================================
— END OF BOOK ONE —
